The Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Forcing Sale from Hoarders
Intra-Hanafi dispute on forcing sale from hoarders

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the difference between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding forcing a hoarder to sell, and state the mufta bihi.

Core Idea
Imam Abu Hanifa forbids forcing a sale; the Sahibayn allow it; later Hanafi consensus permits it to prevent public harm.

This passage continues the discussion on price fixing, focusing on the remedy when hoarders refuse to sell. While Imam Abu Hanifa does not recognize interdiction (hajr) on a free person, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad allow the ruler to sell the hoarder's food. The later mashayikh adopted the latter view due to public necessity.

Guided Path
1
State the issue
The issue is whether the ruler can force a hoarder to sell his goods if he refuses to sell at a fair price.
2
Present Imam Abu Hanifa's position
Imam Abu Hanifa says the ruler cannot force a sale because he does not recognize interdiction (hajr) on a free, adult, sane person. The ruler cannot sell his property against his will.
3
Present the Sahibayn's position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say the ruler can force a sale because they recognize interdiction for the sake of preventing public harm. The hoarder's refusal causes general harm.
4
Describe the later consensus
In al-Muhit, some mashayikh stated that all three (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) agree that the ruler can sell the hoarder's food in cases of necessity. This view became mufta bihi.
The principle: when general and specific harm coincide, repelling general harm is prioritized.
5
Explain the practical consequence
The ruler can sell the hoarder's food, and the sale is valid even if the hoarder is coerced. The buyer's purchase is valid but must avoid coercion if possible.
A hila (device) is suggested: the buyer should say 'Sell me at what you wish' to avoid coercion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله: (ولا يسعر السلطان إلا أن يتعدى أرباب الطعام عن القيمة تعدياً فاحشاً لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام «لا تسعروا فإن الله هو المسعر القابض الباسط الرازق (۳) ولأن الثمن حق البائع وكان إليه تقديره فلا ينبغي للإمام أن يتعرض لحقه إلا إذا كان أرباب الطعام يحتكرون على المسلمين ويتعدون في القيمة تعدياً فاحشاً عن منعه إلا بالتسعير بمشاورة أهل الرأي والنظر، وعجز السلطان لأن فيه ضرراً عاماً . وهذا لا يشكل على قول الأمام لأنه لا يرى الحجر على الحر، وكذا عندهما إلا أن يكون الحجر على قوم بأعيانهم. وينبغي للقاضي وللسلطان أن لا يعجل بعقوبة من باع فوق ما سعر بل يعظه ويزجره وإن رفع إليه ثانياً فعل به كذلك وهدده، وإن رفع إليه ثالثاً حبسه وعزره حتى يمتنع عنه ويمتنع الضرر عن الناس. وفي العتابي : ولو باع شيئاً بثمن زائد على ما قدره الإمام فليس على الإمام أن ينقضه . والغبن الفاحش هو أن يبيعه بضعف قيمته وإذا امتنع أرباب الطعام عن بيعه لا يبيعه القاضي أو السلطان عند الإمام، وعندهما يبيع بناء على أنه لا يرى الحجر على الحر البالغ العاقل وهما يريانه. امتنع المحتكر من بيع الطعام للإمام أن يبيعه عليه عندهم جميعاً على مسألة الحجر، وقيل يبيع بالإجماع لأنه اجتمع ضرر عام وضرر خاص فيقدم دفع الضرر العام كما بينا في كتاب الحجر، قال في المحيط قال بعض مشايخنا : إذا امتنع المحتكر عن بيع الطعام يبيعه الإمام عليه عندهم جميعاً اهـ. ومن باع منهم بما قدره الإمام صح مكره على البيع؛ كذا في الهداية وفي المحيط : إن كان البائع يخاف إذا زاد في الثمن على ما قدره أو نقص في البيع يضربه الإمام أو من يقوم مقامه لا يحل للمشتري ذلك لأنه في معنى المكره، والحيلة في ذلك أن يقول تبيعني بما تحب. ولو اصطلح أهل بلدة على سعر الخبز واللحم وشاع ذلك عندهم فاشترى منهم رجل خبزاً بدرهم أو لحماً بدرهم وأعطاه البائع ناقصاً والمشتري لا يعرف ذلك كان له أن يرجع بالنقصان إذا عرفه لأن المعروف كالمشروط، وإن كان من غير أهل تلك البلد كان له أن بالنقصان في الخبز دون اللحم لأن الخبز يظهر عادة في البلدان وسعر اللحم لا يظهر إلا نادراً فيكون شارطاً في الخبز مقداراً معيناً دون اللحم. ولو خاف الإمام على أهل مصر الهلاك أخذ الطعام من المحتكرين وفرقه، فإذا وجدوه ردوا مثله وليس هذا من باب الحجر وإنما ومن باب دفع الضرر عنهم . حال المخمصة؛ ذكره في شرح المختار
He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And the ruler should not fix prices unless the food sellers exceed the value grossly) due to his saying peace be upon him: 'Do not fix prices, for Allah is the Fixon, the Withholder, the Expander, the Provider.' And because the price is the right of the seller, and its estimation is up to him, so the ruler should not interfere with his right unless the food sellers are hoarding against the Muslims and exceeding the value grossly. Preventing them is only through price fixing with consultation of people of opinion and foresight, and the ruler's inability because there is general harm. This is not problematic on the saying of the Imam because he does not see interdiction (hajr) on a free person; similarly according to the two except if the interdiction is on specific individuals. It is befitting for the judge and ruler not to hasten to punish one who sells above the fixed price, but to admonish and deter him; if he is brought again, he does likewise and threatens him; if brought a third time, he imprisons and disciplines him until he desists and harm is removed from the people. In al-Utabi: If someone sold something at a price exceeding what the ruler fixed, the ruler is not required to nullify it. Gross exploitation (ghabn fahish) is that he sells it at double its value. If the food sellers refuse to sell, the ruler or judge does not sell it according to Imam; according to the two, he sells because he does not see interdiction on a free, adult, sane person, while they do see it. If the hoarder refuses to sell food, the ruler can sell it according to all due to the issue of interdiction; and it is said he sells by consensus because general and specific harm coincide, so repelling general harm is prioritized, as we explained in the book of interdiction. In al-Muhit, some of our teachers said: If the hoarder refuses to sell food, the ruler sells it according to all. And if one of them sells at the price fixed by the ruler, the sale is valid while he is coerced; likewise in al-Hidayah. And in al-Muhit: If the seller fears that if he increases the price above the fixed one or decreases in the sale, the ruler or his deputy will beat him, it is not permissible for the buyer (to accept that) because it is tantamount to coercion. The device (hila) is that he says: 'Sell me at what you wish.' If the people of a town agree on a price for bread and meat and it becomes common among them, then a man buys bread or meat from them for a dirham and the seller gives him less and the buyer does not know that, he has the right to claim the deficiency when he knows it, because what is customary is like a condition. If he is not from that town, he has the right to claim the deficiency in bread but not in meat, because bread's price is commonly known in towns, while meat's price is rarely known, so he is stipulating a specific amount for bread but not for meat. If the ruler fears destruction for the people of a city, he takes food from the hoarders and distributes it; when they find (the owner) they return the like. This is not from the chapter of interdiction but from the chapter of repelling harm from them in a state of famine; as mentioned in Sharh al-Mukhtar.
Source pages p. 370

Commentary
This passage covers the Hanafi ruling on price fixing (tas'ir). The general principle is that the ruler should not fix prices, based on the hadith that Allah alone is the one who sets prices. The price is the seller's right. However, an exception exists when food sellers grossly exceed the market value (ghabn fahish) or hoard, causing public harm. In such cases, the ruler may fix prices after consultation. The commentary details the procedure: first admonition, then threat, then imprisonment. It also discusses differences between Imam Abu Hanifa and the two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) regarding whether the ruler can force a sale from a hoarder: Imam does not allow it (as he does not recognize interdiction on a free person), while the two do. However, a later opinion in al-Muhit says all agree the ruler can sell the hoarder's food. The text also addresses cases where a buyer receives less than the agreed amount due to common price custom, and the case of famine where the ruler can distribute food and the owner is later compensated.

Key Terms
حَجْر (hajr): Interdiction: legal restriction on a person's right to dispose of property due to incapacity or public interest.
مُحْتَكِر (muhtakir): Hoarder: one who withholds goods from the market to drive up prices.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi, can the ruler sell the hoarder's food?
A) No, because of Imam Abu Hanifa's view
B) Yes, by consensus in cases of necessity
C) Yes, but only if the hoarder agrees
D) Never

Key Takeaway
The mufta bihi is that the ruler can sell the hoarder's food to prevent public harm, even though Imam Abu Hanifa originally held otherwise.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
6 min
Custom as Condition and the Ruler's Emergency Powers
Rulings on custom as condition and famine emergency

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that custom is like a condition in price disputes and identify the ruler's authority to distribute food in famine.

Core Idea
Customary prices become a condition; in famine, the ruler can take hoarded food and later compensate owners.

This passage addresses two distinct situations: first, when a town has a customary price for bread and meat, a buyer who receives less than expected can claim the deficiency based on custom. Second, in a famine, the ruler can seize hoarded food and distribute it, with later compensation to the owners. Both rulings derive from the principle of repelling harm.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule
The rule is: 'What is customary is like a condition' (al-ma'ruf ka al-mashrut). When a town agrees on a customary price, that custom becomes an implied condition in all transactions.
2
Apply to bread
Bread prices are commonly known within a town. If a buyer (especially a foreigner) receives less bread than the customary amount for a dirham, he can claim the deficiency because he implicitly stipulated the customary weight.
For a local buyer, the custom is also known, so he has the right as well.
3
Apply to meat
Meat prices vary widely and are not as easily known. Therefore, a foreign buyer cannot claim deficiency based on custom unless he explicitly stipulated a quantity.
The illa (reason) is that meat prices are not commonly known outside the town.
4
Describe the famine exception
If the ruler fears destruction (e.g., famine), he can take food from hoarders and distribute it to the people. This is not considered interdiction (hajr) but rather repelling general harm.
5
Note compensation
When the owners are found later, they are compensated with the like (mithl) of what was taken. This is a temporary measure to save lives.
This is from the chapter of daf' al-darar (repelling harm), not from hajr.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله: (ولا يسعر السلطان إلا أن يتعدى أرباب الطعام عن القيمة تعدياً فاحشاً لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام «لا تسعروا فإن الله هو المسعر القابض الباسط الرازق (۳) ولأن الثمن حق البائع وكان إليه تقديره فلا ينبغي للإمام أن يتعرض لحقه إلا إذا كان أرباب الطعام يحتكرون على المسلمين ويتعدون في القيمة تعدياً فاحشاً عن منعه إلا بالتسعير بمشاورة أهل الرأي والنظر، وعجز السلطان لأن فيه ضرراً عاماً . وهذا لا يشكل على قول الأمام لأنه لا يرى الحجر على الحر، وكذا عندهما إلا أن يكون الحجر على قوم بأعيانهم. وينبغي للقاضي وللسلطان أن لا يعجل بعقوبة من باع فوق ما سعر بل يعظه ويزجره وإن رفع إليه ثانياً فعل به كذلك وهدده، وإن رفع إليه ثالثاً حبسه وعزره حتى يمتنع عنه ويمتنع الضرر عن الناس. وفي العتابي : ولو باع شيئاً بثمن زائد على ما قدره الإمام فليس على الإمام أن ينقضه . والغبن الفاحش هو أن يبيعه بضعف قيمته وإذا امتنع أرباب الطعام عن بيعه لا يبيعه القاضي أو السلطان عند الإمام، وعندهما يبيع بناء على أنه لا يرى الحجر على الحر البالغ العاقل وهما يريانه. امتنع المحتكر من بيع الطعام للإمام أن يبيعه عليه عندهم جميعاً على مسألة الحجر، وقيل يبيع بالإجماع لأنه اجتمع ضرر عام وضرر خاص فيقدم دفع الضرر العام كما بينا في كتاب الحجر، قال في المحيط قال بعض مشايخنا : إذا امتنع المحتكر عن بيع الطعام يبيعه الإمام عليه عندهم جميعاً اهـ. ومن باع منهم بما قدره الإمام صح مكره على البيع؛ كذا في الهداية وفي المحيط : إن كان البائع يخاف إذا زاد في الثمن على ما قدره أو نقص في البيع يضربه الإمام أو من يقوم مقامه لا يحل للمشتري ذلك لأنه في معنى المكره، والحيلة في ذلك أن يقول تبيعني بما تحب. ولو اصطلح أهل بلدة على سعر الخبز واللحم وشاع ذلك عندهم فاشترى منهم رجل خبزاً بدرهم أو لحماً بدرهم وأعطاه البائع ناقصاً والمشتري لا يعرف ذلك كان له أن يرجع بالنقصان إذا عرفه لأن المعروف كالمشروط، وإن كان من غير أهل تلك البلد كان له أن بالنقصان في الخبز دون اللحم لأن الخبز يظهر عادة في البلدان وسعر اللحم لا يظهر إلا المعروف كالمشروط (al-ma'ruf ka al-mashrut): A widely known custom becomes an implied condition in contracts.
مَخْمَصَة (makhmasa): Famine; scarcity of food causing fear of death.

Ruling on Selling Grape Juice to a Wine Merchant
Permits sale of grape juice to wine merchant with qiyas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling grape juice to a wine merchant and apply the underlying principle of sin inherence to distinguish permissible from impermissible sales.

Core Idea
A sale is permissible if the sin does not inhere in the object but stems from the buyer's choice.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a common question: can you sell grape juice to someone who will make it into wine? The Hanafi school permits it, drawing a careful distinction between objects inherently tied to sin and those that become sinful only through human action. We'll explore the reasoning and its applications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is selling grape juice ('asir) to a wine merchant (khammar). The ruling is permissible (ja'iz, mubah).
The text opens with 'وجاز بيع العصير من خمار'.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is qiyas (analogy). The effective cause ('illa) is whether sin is inherent in the object itself.
Contrast with selling weapons to rebels, where sin is inherent.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
For grape juice, sin does not inhere in the juice but occurs after it changes into wine. For weapons, sin inheres in the weapon itself. Therefore, selling juice is not assisting sin, while selling weapons is.
The text cites the Quranic prohibition on cooperating in sin.
4
Apply the principle to other examples
The passage extends the principle: selling a silver-plated cup to men (who will wear it) is disliked if known; making shoes in sinful styles is disliked. The shift to 'disliked' (karaha) occurs when the object's use is more directly tied to sin.
These are applications of the same 'illa, but with degrees of certainty.
5
Evaluate the cross-madhhab status
The ruling is mukhtalaf fih; some schools may prohibit aiding sin. The Hanafi position relies on the object's intrinsic neutrality.
Understanding this helps appreciate the school's method.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : ( وجاز بيع العصير من خمار) لأن المعصية لا تقوم بعينه بل بعد تغيره بخلاف بيع السلاح من أهل الفتنة لأن المعصية تقوم بعينه فيكون إعانة لهم وتسبباً وقد نهينا عن التعاون على العدوان والمعصية، ولأن العصير يصلح للأشياء كلها جائزة شرعاً فيكون الفساد إلى اختياره وبيع المكعب المفضض للرجال إذا علم أنه يشتريه ليلبسه يكره لأنه إعانة له على لبس الحرام ولو أن اسكافياً أمره إنسان أن يتخذ له خفاً على زي المجوس أو الفسقة أو خياطاً أمره إنسان أن يخيط له قميصاً على زي الفساق يكره له أن يفعل ذلك؛ كذا في المحيط .
He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And it is permissible to sell juice [grape juice] to a wine merchant) because the disobedience does not inhere in the juice itself but after its change, unlike selling weapons to people of sedition, because disobedience inheres in the weapon itself, so it is assistance to them and causing, and we have been forbidden from cooperating in aggression and sin. And because juice is suitable for all things that are legally permissible, so corruption is due to his choice. And selling a silver-plated drinking vessel (muka'ab mufaddad) to men, if he knows he will buy it to wear, is disliked because it assists him in wearing the forbidden. And if a cobbler is ordered by a person to make a shoe in the style of the Magians or sinful people, or a tailor is ordered to sew a shirt in the style of sinful people, it is disliked for him to do that; likewise in al-Muhit.
Source pages p. 371

Commentary
This passage rules on the permissibility of selling grape juice ('asir) to a wine merchant (khammar). The reasoning is that the juice itself is not an instrument of sin; sin only occurs after it changes into wine. This is contrasted with selling weapons to rebels, where the weapon is inherently used for sin, thus prohibited. Further examples illustrate the principle: selling a silver-plated cup to a man who will wear it (since men wearing silver is haram) is disliked if the seller knows. Similarly, making shoes in the style of non-Muslims or sinners is disliked if requested. The key distinction is whether the object is inherently tied to sin or becomes sinful only through the buyer's choice.

Key Terms
عصير (asir): Grape juice or any fruit juice that can become alcoholic.
خمار (khammar): Wine merchant or someone who makes wine.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following best explains why selling grape juice to a wine merchant is permissible in the Hanafi school?
A) Because juice is not yet intoxicating
B) Because sin does not inhere in the juice itself
C) Because the merchant is a Muslim
D) Because the sale is a valid contract

Key Takeaway
Selling grape juice to a wine merchant is permissible because sin does not inhere in the juice; the prohibition applies only when the object is inherently tied to sin.

Rulings on Price Fixing, Hoarding, and Excessive Overpricing
Analyzes ruler's powers in price fixing, forced sale, and excessive overpricing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi intra-madhhab positions on forced sale due to price fixing and hoarding, and identify the definition of ghubn fahish.

Core Idea
The ruler may fix prices, but forcing a sale depends on whether the seller is a hoarder or not, and on the Imam's view on hajr.

This lesson explores the Hanafi rulings on price fixing (tas‘īr), forced sale, and excessive overpricing (ghubn fāḥish). It highlights the difference between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Ṣāḥibayn regarding the ruler's power to compel a sale, and the consensus on hoarding. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for applying fiqh to market regulation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key actors
Determine who is involved: the ruler, the seller (ordinary supplier or hoarder), and the buyer.
The passage distinguishes between ordinary food suppliers and hoarders.
2
Understand the dispute on forced sale
Imam Abu Hanifa does not allow forced sale for ordinary suppliers due to his view on hajr; the Ṣāḥibayn allow it. Note the exception for hoarders where all agree.
Hajr is the legal restriction on a person's capacity.
3
Learn the escalation procedure
The ruler should admonish, then warn, then imprison a seller who violates fixed pricing.
This is the recommended procedure before any coercive action.
4
Define ghubn fahish
Excessive overpricing is defined as selling at double the market value.
This entitles the buyer to rescind the contract if unaware.
5
Apply the rulings to hoarding
All Hanafi imams agree the ruler can force a hoarder to sell, prioritizing public harm over private right.
Public harm overrides private harm in cases of scarcity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإذا فعل ذلك على رجل فتعدى وباع بثمن فوقه أجازه القاضي، وهذا لا يشكل على قول الأمام لأنه لا يرى الحجر على الحر، وكذا عندهما إلا أن يكون الحجر على قوم بأعيانهم. وينبغي للقاضي وللسلطان أن لا يعجل بعقوبة من باع فوق ما سعر بل يعظه ويزجره وإن رفع إليه ثانياً فعل به كذلك وهدده، وإن رفع إليه ثالثاً حبسه وعزره حتى يمتنع عنه ويمتنع الضرر عن الناس. وفي العتابي : ولو باع شيئاً بثمن زائد على ما قدره الإمام فليس على الإمام أن ينقضه . والغبن الفاحش هو أن يبيعه بضعف قيمته وإذا امتنع أرباب الطعام عن بيعه لا يبيعه القاضي أو السلطان عند الإمام، وعندهما يبيع بناء على أنه لا يرى الحجر على الحر البالغ العاقل وهما يريانه. امتنع المحتكر من بيع الطعام للإمام أن يبيعه عليه عندهم جميعاً على مسألة الحجر، وقيل يبيع بالإجماع لأنه اجتمع ضرر عام وضرر خاص فيقدم دفع الضرر العام كما بينا في كتاب الحجر، قال في المحيط قال بعض مشايخنا : إذا امتنع المحتكر عن بيع الطعام يبيعه الإمام عليه عندهم جميعاً اهـ. ومن باع منهم بما قدره الإمام صح مكره على البيع؛ كذا في الهداية وفي المحيط : إن كان البائع يخاف إذا زاد في الثمن على ما قدره أو نقص في البيع يضربه الإمام أو من يقوم مقامه لا يحل للمشتري ذلك لأنه في معنى المكره، والحيلة في ذلك أن يقول تبيعني بما تحب. ولو اصطلح أهل بلدة على سعر الخبز واللحم وشاع ذلك عندهم فاشترى منهم رجل خبزاً بدرهم أو لحماً بدرهم وأعطاه البائع ناقصاً والمشتري لا يعرف ذلك كان له أن يرجع بالنقصان إذا عرفه لأن المعروف كالمشروط، وإن كان من غير تلك البلد كان له أن بالنقصان في الخبز دون اللحم لأن الخبز يظهر عادة في البلدان وسعر اللحم لا يظهر إلا نادراً فيكون شارطاً في الخبز مقداراً معيناً دون اللحم. ولو خاف الإمام على أهل مصر الهلاك أخذ الطعام من المحتكرين وفرقه، فإذا وجدوه ردوا مثله وليس هذا من باب الحجر وإنما ومن باب دفع الضرر عنهم . حال المخمصة؛ ذكره في شرح المختار
If a man does that, transgresses, and sells at a price above it, the judge validates it. This is not problematic on the statement of the Imam because he does not see restriction (hajr) on a free person. Likewise according to both (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) except that the restriction is on specific individuals. It is befitting for the judge and the ruler not to hasten punishment on one who sold above what was fixed, but rather to admonish and rebuke him. If it is raised to him a second time, he does likewise and threatens him. If it is raised to him a third time, he imprisons and disciplines him until he desists and prevents harm from the people. In al-‘Utābi: If one sold something at a price exceeding what the Imam fixed, the Imam is not obligated to annul it. Excessive overpricing (ghubn fāḥish) is that he sells it at double its value. If the food suppliers refuse to sell it, the judge or ruler does not sell it according to the Imam, but according to both (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) he sells, based on the fact that he (Imam) does not see restriction on a free, adult, sane person while they (the two) see it. (Regarding) the hoarder who refuses to sell food: the Imam sells it from him according to all of them on the matter of restriction, and it is said he sells by consensus because public harm and private harm combine, so repelling public harm is given precedence as we explained in the Book of Restriction. He said in al-Muḥīṭ: Some of our teachers said: If the hoarder refuses to sell food, the Imam sells it from him according to all of them, end quote. Whoever among them sells at what the Imam fixed, it is valid, though he is coerced into selling; thus in al-Hidāya. In al-Muḥīṭ: If the seller fears that if he increases the price above what was fixed or decreases in the sale, the Imam or his deputy will strike him, it is not permissible for the buyer to do so because it is in the meaning of coercion. The legal trick is for him to say: Sell me at whatever you like. If the people of a town agreed on a price for bread and meat, and it became common among them, and a man from them bought bread for a dirham or meat for a dirham, and the seller gave him less while the buyer does not know, he has the right to claim the deficiency when he comes to know, because what is known is like a condition. But if he is not from that town, he has the right to claim deficiency for bread but not for meat, because bread is usually known in towns whereas the price of meat is rarely known. If the Imam fears destruction for the people of a territory, he takes food from the hoarders and distributes it; when they find (the food), they return its like. This is not from the chapter of restriction but from the chapter of repelling harm from them in times of famine. It is mentioned in Sharḥ al-Mukhtār.
Source pages p. 371

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute and ruling regarding price fixing (tas‘īr), excessive overpricing (ghubn fāḥish), and hoarding (iḥtikār). First, it addresses a seller who sells above the price fixed by the ruler. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the judge validates the sale because the Imam does not uphold placing a restriction (ḥajr) on a free person's ability to transact. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Ṣāḥibayn) also generally agree, except when the restriction is on a specific group. The recommended procedure for the ruler is to admonish a first-time offender, warn a second-time, and imprison and discipline a third-time offender. Al-‘Utābi reports that if a sale exceeds the fixed price, the Imam is not required to annul it. Next, it defines excessive overpricing as selling at double the value. Regarding food suppliers who refuse to sell, Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the ruler cannot force the sale, while the Ṣāḥibayn allow it because they recognize restriction on a free person. However, for a hoarder (muḥtakir), all agree the ruler can force the sale, as public harm outweighs private harm. The commentary from al-Muḥīṭ confirms this consensus. If a hoarder sells at the fixed price, the sale is valid despite coercion. If the seller is coerced by fear of beating, the buyer should not proceed. A legal trick (ḥīla) is for the buyer to say 'Sell me at whatever you like.' Finally, it discusses buying bread or meat at a fixed price with deficiency. If the buyer is from the same town, he can claim the deficiency because the common price is implied; if not, only bread (whose price is widely known) is covered.

Key Terms
التسعير (al-tas‘īr): The fixing of prices by the ruler to prevent harm.
الحجر (al-ḥajr): A legal restriction placed on a person's capacity to transact.
الغبن الفاحش (al-ghubn al-fāḥish): Excessive overpricing defined as selling at double the market value.
المحتكر (al-muḥtakir): One who withholds food from the market expecting a price rise.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling regarding the hoarder (muhtakir) who refuses to sell food?
A) The ruler cannot force the sale because of hajr.
B) The ruler can force the sale according to all Hanafi imams.
C) Only Imam Abu Hanifa allows forced sale of the hoarder.
D) The Sahibayn do not allow forced sale of the hoarder.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the ruler can set prices but forced sale depends on the seller's status and the level of public harm, with hoarders being subject to forced sale by all imams.

Selling Grape Juice to a Wine-Maker and the Principle of Non-Cooperation in Sin
Explains permissibility with dalil and extends the principle to analogous cases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that sin attaches to an object's essence to distinguish permissible sales from prohibited cooperation.

Core Idea
Sin that inheres in the object's essence prohibits the sale; sin from change or use does not.

In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi ruling on selling grape juice to a wine-maker. The key is whether sin attaches to the object's essence or occurs only after a change. This principle also governs sales of weapons to rebels, silver vessels to men, and making garments in immoral styles.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and the act
The author states it is permissible (jā'iz) to sell grape juice to a wine-maker. This is the ruling we will analyze.
The ruling is explicitly stated in the matn.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is the Quranic principle: 'Do not cooperate in sin and transgression' (Quran 5:2). The commentary uses this to distinguish cases where sin inheres in the object.
The dalil is implicit; the text references the meaning.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Sin does not inhere in grape juice because it becomes wine only after a change (fermentation). Thus, selling the juice is not cooperating in sin. This contrasts with weapons, whose primary use is harmful, so sin inheres.
The distinction is based on the essence (ʿayn) of the object.
4
Apply the principle to other cases
The text extends the principle: selling a silver-inlaid cube to a man is makruh if the seller knows it will be worn, because using silver is forbidden for men. Similarly, making footwear or clothing in immoral styles is makruh.
These are applications of the same principle of assisting sin.
5
Evaluate the ruling's scope
The permissibility applies when the object has permissible uses and the sin depends on the buyer's choice. If the object's primary use is unlawful, the sale is prohibited.
This is the criterion for distinguishing direct vs. indirect assistance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : ( وجاز بيع العصير من خمار) لأن المعصية لا تقوم بعينه بل بعد تغيره بخلاف بيع السلاح من أهل الفتنة لأن المعصية تقوم بعينه فيكون إعانة لهم وتسبباً وقد نهينا عن التعاون على العدوان والمعصية، ولأن العصير يصلح للأشياء كلها جائزة شرعاً فيكون الفساد إلى اختياره وبيع المكعب المفضض للرجال إذا علم أنه يشتريه ليلبسه يكره لأنه إعانة له على لبس الحرام ولو أن اسكافياً أمره إنسان أن يتخذ له خفاً على زي المجوس أو الفسقة أو خياطاً أمره إنسان أن يخيط له قميصاً على زي الفساق يكره له أن يفعل ذلك؛ كذا في المحيط .
He (the author) said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And it is permissible to sell grape juice to a wine-maker) because sin does not inhere in its essence but after it changes, unlike selling weapons to people of discord because sin inheres in its essence, thus it is assistance to them and a means, and we have been forbidden to cooperate in injustice and sin. And because grape juice is suitable for all things permissible by law, so corruption is left to his choice. And selling a silver-inlaid cube (muka‘ab mufaḍḍad) to men, if one knows that he buys it to wear it, is disliked (makrūh) because it is assistance to him in wearing the forbidden. And if a cobbler is ordered by a person to make him a khuff in the style of the Magians or transgressors, or a tailor is ordered by a person to sew him a shirt in the style of transgressors, it is disliked for him to do that; thus in al-Muḥīṭ.
Source pages p. 371

Commentary
This passage provides a ruling with proof on selling grape juice to a wine-maker. The author (صاحب الكنز) states it is permissible. The commentary explains the reasoning: sin (ma‘ṣiyah) does not attach to the wine itself—it is permissible grape juice that only becomes wine after a change (fermentation). This contrasts with selling weapons to rebels or oppressors, where sin inheres in the weapon itself because its primary use is to cause harm. Selling weapons in such a case is an aid to transgression, which is prohibited. Grape juice, however, has many permissible uses (drinking, cooking, etc.), so the sin depends on the buyer's choice. The commentary then extends the principle to other cases: selling a silver-inlaid cube (a type of ornament) to men is disliked (makrūh) if the seller knows the man will wear it, as wearing silver is unlawful for men. Similarly, a cobbler or tailor should not make footwear or clothing in the style of immoral people if that would be assisting in sin.

Key Terms
المعصية (al-ma‘ṣiyah): Sin or disobedience to Allah; in this context, the sin of producing or consuming wine.
المكعب المفضض (al-muka‘ab al-mufaḍḍad): A cube-shaped object inlaid with silver, possibly a drinking vessel or ornament.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is selling grape juice to a wine-maker permissible?
A) Because the wine-maker will not necessarily make wine.
B) Because sin does not inhere in the juice itself; it only becomes wine after change.
C) Because the seller is not responsible for the buyer's actions.
D) Both B and C.

Key Takeaway
The sale of an object is permissible as long as sin is not inherent in its essence, even if the buyer will use it for sin.

Building and Selling Land in Mecca – Hanafi Ruling
Ruling on constructing buildings and selling land in Mecca

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on building and selling land in Mecca and identify the proof from the hadith of ‘Aqīl.

Core Idea
Land in Mecca is owned and transferable; building and selling are permissible.

A common misunderstanding is that Mecca's sanctity prohibits land transactions. This passage clarifies the Hanafi position: building is permissible and the builder owns the structure regardless of the land's status; selling land is valid because ownership is established through customary practice (ta‘āruf) and a Prophetic indication. The relied-upon view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad allows sale, supported by the hadith of ‘Aqīl.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
The passage addresses two separate issues: building houses in Mecca and selling its land. Note that the ruling for building is explicitly stated as permissible. For selling, the text presents the view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad.
Building is permissible regardless of land ownership (owned, leased, waqf). Selling requires ownership of the land.
2
Locate the proof for selling
The author cites the hadith: 'Has ‘Aqīl left us any houses?' This indicates that land was owned and inherited, thus saleable. Customary practice (ta‘āruf) also supports ownership.
The hadith is from Sahih al-Bukhari (hadith of ‘Aqīl). The word 'ribā‘' means estates/houses.
3
Understand the intra-Hanafi difference
There is an alternative narration from Abu Hanifa that land in Mecca is not owned (possibly waqf). However, the view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad is adopted as the muftā bihī (operative ruling).
The text does not discuss the proof for the other narration.
4
Apply to a modern scenario
If a person buys a plot in Mecca today, the sale is valid according to the relied-upon Hanafi position. The buyer becomes owner and can build on it.
This ruling applies to both residential and commercial land.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : بناء بيوت مكة أو أراضيها يعني يجوز ذلك، أما البناء فظاهر لأنه ملك لبنائه ألا بنى في المستأجر أو الوقف جاز البناء وكان له ملكاً له، وأما بيع أراضيها فالمذكور هنا قول أبي يوسف ومحمد وهو إحدى الروايتين عن الإمام لأن أراضيها مملوكة لأهلها لظهور التصرف والاختصاص ولقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام هل ترك لنا عقيل من رباع الحديث. فيه دليل على أن أراضيها تملك وتقبل الانتقال من ملك إلى ملك وقد تعارف الناس ذلك من أول (وبيع تری أنه لو
He said, may Allah have mercy on him: Building houses in Mecca or its land: meaning that is permissible. As for building, it is apparent because he owns what he builds; or if he builds on leased or waqf land, the building is permissible and becomes his property. As for selling its land, what is mentioned here is the statement of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, and it is one of the two narrations from the Imam (Abu Hanifa), because its lands are owned by its people due to the appearance of disposition and exclusivity, and due to his saying (peace be upon him): 'Has ‘Aqīl left us any houses?' The hadith (continues). In it is evidence that its lands are owned and accept transfer from one ownership to another, and people have customarily done that from the beginning.
Source pages p. 372

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling on building houses and selling land in Mecca. The matn allows it. The commentary explains: Building on owned land is permissible because the builder owns the structure. Even if built on leased or waqf land, the building is permissible and becomes the builder's property. For selling land, the view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad is presented, which is also one of two narrations from Imam Abu Hanifa. The proof is that people in Mecca have always transacted in land, and the hadith of ‘Aqīl (the Prophet's cousin) indicates that land was owned and inherited. The text is cut off but likely continues to support the permissibility of selling Meccan land.

Key Terms
رباع (ribā‘): Houses or estates; specifically the properties left by ‘Aqīl in Mecca.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Consensus of the Companions
B) The hadith about ‘Aqīl’s houses
C) The principle of necessity
D) A Quranic verse

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school, relying on the hadith of ‘Aqīl and custom, rules that land in Mecca is owned and can be freely sold.

Carrying the Wine of a Dhimmi for Hire: An Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Ruling with intra-Hanafi dispute on carrying dhimmi's wine for hire and related cases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on carrying dhimmi wine for hire and apply the principle of intention to related cases.

Core Idea
Carrying wine for a dhimmi is not inherently sinful; the ruling depends on the carrier's intention.

In this passage, the author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents a nuanced ruling on hiring oneself or one's animal to carry wine owned by a dhimmi. The central disagreement within the Hanafi school—between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two companions, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani—hinges on how to interpret the prophetic hadith that curses the carrier of wine. Abu Hanifa argues that the contract is on the neutral act of carrying, and sin only arises from intention, while the Sahibayn apply the hadith broadly. The same principle extends to tending pigs, working in a church, and serving a dhimmi in a bathhouse.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the dispute
Recognize that the ruling concerns hiring (ijara) for carrying wine owned by a dhimmi. Note that the disagreement is between Imam Abu Hanifa (permissible) and the Sahibayn (disliked).
The act is carrying, not consuming or selling the wine.
2
Examine Abu Hanifa's proof
Understand his reasoning: the hire contract is on the act of carrying, which is not inherently sinful. Sin is only committed by the independent doer with intention. He analogizes to hiring someone to press grapes or pick them.
He also interprets the hadith as applying only to carriers with sinful intention.
3
Examine the Sahibayn's proof
They rely on the hadith where the Prophet (peace be upon him) cursed ten types of people involved with wine, including the carrier. For them, the carrier is cursed regardless of intention.
The dispute is about the scope of the hadith.
4
Apply the principle to related cases
The same difference applies to hiring an animal to carry wine, hiring oneself to tend pigs, working in a church for a dhimmi, and serving a Jew in a bathhouse. The permissibility depends on the intention: if for worldly gain or da'wa, it is allowed; if out of reverence, it is disliked.
The later sources (Tatarkhaniyya, Dhakhira) affirm the permissibility for worldly motives.
5
Derive the overarching rule
The key principle is that acts of service to dhimmis are judged by the intention behind them. The Hanafi school balances preventing cooperation in sin with allowing lawful worldly transactions and da'wa opportunities.
The mufta bihi (operative ruling) is the permissibility of Imam Abu Hanifa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله تعالى: (وحمل خمر الذمي بأجر) يعني جاز ذلك وهذا عند الإمام. وقالا : يكره لأنه عليه الصلاة والسلام لعن في الخمر عشرة وعد منها حاملها. وله أن الإجارة على الحمل وهو ليس بمعصية وإنما المعصية بفعل فاعل مختار فصار كمن استأجره لعصره خمر العنب وقطفه والحديث يحمل على الحمل المقرون بقصد المعصية وعلى هذا الخلاف إذا أجر دابة ليحمل عليها الخمر أو نفسه ليرعى له الخنازير فإنه يطيب له الأجرة عنده، وعندهما يكره. وفي التتارخانية ولو أجر المسلم نفسه لذمي ليعمل في الكنيسة فلا بأس به. وفي الذخيرة : إذا دخل يهودي الحمام هل يباح للخادم المسلم أن يخدمه قال : إن خدمه طمعاً في فلوسه فلا بأس به، وإن خدمه تعظيماً له ينظر ؛ إن فعل ذلك ليميل قلبه إلى الإسلام فلا بأس به، وإن فعله تعظيماً له كره ذلك، وعلى هذا إذا دخل ذمي على مسلم فقام له طمعاً في إسلامه فلا بأس به، وإن قام له تعظيماً له كره له ذلك.
He (the author) said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And carrying the wine of the dhimmi for hire). Meaning it is permissible, and this is according to Imam (Abu Hanifa). The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: It is disliked because the Prophet (peace be upon him) cursed ten regarding wine, and among them he counted its carrier. For him (Abu Hanifa) is that the contract of hire is upon carrying, and it is not a sin; rather sin is by the act of the independent doer. So it becomes like one who hires him to press grape-wine or to pick grapes. And the hadith is interpreted as the carrying accompanied by the intention of sin. Upon this difference is the case if he hires a beast to carry wine upon it, or hires himself to tend pigs for him – the wage is permissible according to him, and according to both it is disliked. And in al-Tatarkhaniyya: If a Muslim hires himself to a dhimmi to work in the church, there is no harm in it. And in al-Dhakhira: If a Jew enters the bathhouse, is it permissible for the Muslim servant to serve him? He said: If he serves him desiring his money, there is no harm; if he serves him out of reverence for him, then it is considered: if he does that to incline his heart to Islam, there is no harm; but if he does it out of reverence, that is disliked. Upon this, if a dhimmi enters upon a Muslim and he stands up for him out of desire for his conversion to Islam, there is no harm; if he stands up out of reverence for him, that is disliked for him.
Source pages p. 372

Commentary
This passage begins with a new topic: the permissibility of carrying wine owned by a dhimmi for hire. The author states the ruling clearly: it is permissible according to Abu Hanifa. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) consider it disliked (makruh) because of the hadith that the Prophet (peace be upon him) cursed ten people involved with wine, including the carrier. Abu Hanifa's proof is that the contract is on the act of carrying, which is not inherently sinful; sin is only attributable to the independent act of the doer. He analogizes it to hiring someone to press grapes or pick them – these actions are not sinful per se. The hadith is interpreted as applying only when the carrying is accompanied by an intention of sin. The same difference applies if one hires an animal to carry wine or hires himself to tend pigs: the wage is lawful according to Abu Hanifa, disliked according to them. The passage then cites two additional sources (al-Tatarkhaniyya and al-Dhakhira) that discuss related permissibility issues: working in a church for a Muslim servant, and serving a Jew in the bathhouse. These are permitted if done for worldly gain (like money) or for the purpose of calling to Islam, but disliked if done out of reverence for the dhimmi. The same principle applies to standing up for a dhimmi. The commentator integrates these rulings as practical applications of the general principle that acts of service to dhimmis are judged by the intention behind them.

Key Terms
حاملها (hamiluha): The carrier of wine; included among those cursed by the Prophet in the hadith regarding wine.
التعظيم (al-ta'dhim): Reverence or veneration; here it refers to serving or standing up for a dhimmi out of respect for his status, which is disliked.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the difference between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the hired carrier of dhimmi wine?
A) Abu Hanifa says haram, Sahibayn says halal.
B) Abu Hanifa says permissible, Sahibayn says disliked.
C) Both say permissible.
D) Both say haram.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school judges acts of service to dhimmis by intention: permissible if for worldly gain or da'wa, disliked if out of reverence.

Selling Land in Mecca: Hanafi Intra-Madhhab Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on land sale in Mecca

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the positions of Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on selling land in Mecca and identify the strongest evidence for each.

Core Idea
Sale of Meccan land is permissible by the Sahibayn; Imam Abu Hanifa held it is communal property not for sale.

The sanctity of Mecca raises questions about ownership and commerce. While building structures is uncontroversial, selling the land itself sparked internal Hanafi debate. This lesson examines the conflicting proofs and the relied-upon view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the dispute
The passage discusses selling land in Mecca; Abu Hanifa prohibits, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad permit.
This is an intra-madhhab ikhtilaf.
2
Examine the evidence for permissibility
The Sahibayn cite hadith indicating ownership ('Did Aqil leave us houses?') and continuous custom of sale without objection.
Custom (ta'aruf) is a strong proof in Hanafi fiqh.
3
Examine the evidence for prohibition
Abu Hanifa cites hadith that Allah forbade sale and rent of Meccan land, that it is a waqf of Abraham, and the earlier practice of sawa'ib (communal use).
These are dalil naqli (textual proofs).
4
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: if sale is invalid, contracts for land in Mecca are fasid; if valid, they are enforceable.
The mufta bihi is permissibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : بناء بيوت مكة أو أراضيها يعني يجوز ذلك، أما البناء فظاهر لأنه ملك لبنائه ألا بنى في المستأجر أو الوقف جاز البناء وكان له ملكاً له، وأما بيع أراضيها فالمذكور هنا قول أبي يوسف ومحمد وهو إحدى الروايتين عن الإمام لأن أراضيها مملوكة لأهلها لظهور التصرف والاختصاص ولقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام هل ترك لنا عقيل من رباع الحديث. فيه دليل على أن أراضيها تملك وتقبل الانتقال من ملك إلى ملك وقد تعارف الناس ذلك من أول (وبيع تری أنه لو لا يجوز بيع أراضيها الآن من غير نكير وهو من أقوى الحجج. وقال الإمام: الإسلام إلى لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام إن الله حرم مكة وحرم بيع أراضيها وإجارتها» ولأنه وقف الخليل عليه الصلاة والسلام ولأن الأراضي بمكة كانت تدعي في زمن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم والخليفتين من بعده بالسوائب من احتاج إليها سكنها ومن استغنى عنها تركها
He (the author) said, may Allah have mercy on him: (Building houses of Mecca or its lands). Meaning it is permissible. As for building, it is obvious because he owns what he builds; even if he builds in a leased [property] or a waqf, the building is permissible and becomes his property. As for selling its lands, what is mentioned here is the statement of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, which is one of two narrations from Imam (Abu Hanifa) because its lands are owned by their people due to the apparent disposal and specification, and due to his saying (peace be upon him): 'Did Aqil leave us a house?' [hadith]. In it is evidence that its lands are owned and accept transfer from one ownership to another, and people have customarily done so from the beginning [of Islam]. You see that its lands are sold now without any objection, and this is among the strongest proofs. And Imam (Abu Hanifa) said: [The ruling leans] to Islam because of his saying (peace be upon him): 'Indeed Allah has forbidden Mecca and forbidden the sale of its lands and their rent.' And because it is the waqf of al-Khalil (Abraham, peace be upon him), and because the lands in Mecca were claimed in the time of the Prophet (peace be upon him) and the two Caliphs after him as 'sawa'ib' (communal): whoever needed them lived in them and whoever had no need left them.
Source pages p. 372

Commentary
This passage discusses the permissibility of building houses and selling land in Mecca. The author begins by stating that building is clearly permissible because the builder owns the structure. Even if built on leased or waqf land, the building is owned by the builder. The dispute concerns the sale of the land itself. The position mentioned here is that of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (and one narration from Abu Hanifa) that selling is permissible. Their evidence includes: the land is owned by the inhabitants (due to longstanding possession and specific rights), the hadith where the Prophet asked if Aqil had left any houses (implying ownership), and the continuous custom from early times of selling land in Mecca without objection—this custom (ijma' 'urfi) is a strong proof. The author then mentions the opposing view of Imam Abu Hanifa (in another narration) that selling is not permissible. His evidence includes a hadith that Allah made Mecca sacred and prohibited the sale and rent of its lands, the fact that the sanctuary is a waqf of Prophet Abraham (peace be upon him), and the historical practice during the Prophet's time and the Caliphate where land was treated as common property (sawa'ib)—anyone in need would occupy it, and when leaving, they would leave it vacant. The author does not explicitly choose a position but presents both clearly. The later Hanafi position leans toward permissibility as the dominant view.

Key Terms
السوائب (al-sawa'ib): Communal or free lands; lands that are not privately owned but available for anyone in need to use temporarily.
وقف الخليل (waqf al-khalil): The endowment of Prophet Abraham (peace be upon him); according to Abu Hanifa, the sanctity of Mecca makes it a perpetual endowment for the public.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) A hadith that the Prophet prohibited selling land in Mecca.
B) The continuous custom of people selling land in Mecca without objection since early times.
C) The fact that the land is waqf of Abraham.
D) The verse from Surah al-Ma'idah.

Key Takeaway
The majority Hanafi position permits selling land in Mecca based on custom and hadith, while Imam Abu Hanifa's prohibition is based on sacredness; the relied-upon view is permissibility.

Depositing a Dirham with a Shopkeeper: The Ruling and the Correct Alternative
Ruling on a financial arrangement and its correction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on placing a dirham with a shopkeeper to take goods at will, explain why it is prohibited as qard jarra naf'an, and distinguish it from the valid deposit arrangement.

Core Idea
A loan that gives the lender a benefit is riba — even if it looks like convenient shopping.

Many people think leaving money with a shopkeeper to grab goods as needed is just convenient. But the Hanafi scholars point out a hidden problem: the money becomes a loan, and the right to take goods becomes a benefit for the lender. This is actually a prohibited transaction, akin to usury. In this lesson, we examine the ruling and the correct alternative.

Guided Path
1
Identify the transaction
What is the person doing? He places a dirham with a shopkeeper and takes goods from the shop as he wishes, up to the value of the dirham.
This is a common arrangement, but its legal nature must be analyzed.
2
Analyze the legal nature
Once the dirham is handed over, it becomes the property of the shopkeeper. In exchange, the person has a right to take goods. This makes the dirham effectively a loan (qard) to the shopkeeper.
The dirham is not a deposit; it is a loan because the shopkeeper can use it.
3
Identify the stipulation
The person stipulates that he can take goods from the shop. This is a benefit that flows back to the lender (the customer). The benefit is the ability to obtain goods without paying each time, and the convenience of having the dirham available for future purchases.
Any benefit to the lender from a loan is prohibited.
4
Apply the principle
The transaction falls under 'qard jarra naf'an' (a loan that draws a benefit). This is prohibited in the Hanafi school, and the commentator rules it as disliked (makruh tahrimi), meaning it is close to haram.
The hadith: 'Every loan that draws a benefit is riba.'
5
Learn the correct alternative
The commentator advises that the money should be placed as a deposit (wadi'ah) — a trust — not as a loan. Then the person takes goods little by little. If the money is lost, the shopkeeper is not liable because it is an amanah.
This avoids the prohibited benefit while achieving the same practical outcome.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشارح: ومن وضع عند بقال درهماً يأخذ منه ما شاء كره له ذلك لأنه إذا ملكه الدرهم فقد أقرضه إياه وقد شرط أن يأخذ منه من القبول وغيرها ما شاء وله في ذلك نفع بقاء الدرهم وكفايته للحاجات، ولو نفعاً كان في يده لخرج من ساعته ولم يبق فصار في معنى قرض جر وهو منهي عنه، وينبغي يودعه عنده ثم يأخذ منه شيئاً فشيئاً، وإن ضاع فلا شيء عليه لأن الوديعة أمانة ا هـ.
The commentator said: And whoever places a dirham with a shopkeeper, taking from him whatever he wishes, it is disliked for him. Because when he makes the dirham his property, he has indeed loaned it to him, and he has stipulated that he takes from him from the acceptance[?] and other things whatever he wishes. And he has a benefit in that by the dirham remaining and sufficing for needs. Even if there was a benefit in his hand, it would leave him immediately and not remain. So it falls under the meaning of a loan that draws benefit (qard jarra naf'an), which is prohibited. He should instead deposit it with him as an amanah (trust), then take from it little by little, and if it is lost, there is nothing upon him because the deposit is a trust. End of quote.
Source pages p. 373

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory note from the commentator (likely Ibn Nujaym) on a financial transaction. It describes a scenario where a person places a dirham with a shopkeeper, with the understanding that he can take any goods he wants from the shop against that dirham. The commentator says this is disliked (makruh) because the dirham effectively becomes a loan (qard) to the shopkeeper once it is handed over, and the person stipulates that he can take goods from the shop. This stipulation generates a benefit for the lender (the dirham remains available for his needs), which makes it a 'loan that draws benefit' (qard jarra naf'an), which is prohibited in hadith. The legal problem is that the lender retains a beneficial right (access to goods) while the shopkeeper owes the debt. The solution is to treat the dirham as a deposit (wadi'ah) instead: the customer leaves the money as an amanah, and then takes goods gradually. If the money is lost, the shopkeeper is not liable because it is a trust. This ruling illustrates the Hanafi principle that any loan that provides a benefit to the lender is usurious.

Key Terms
قرض جر (qard jarra): A loan that draws a benefit to the lender; here, the lender gets the ability to take goods, which is a benefit.
الوديعة (al-wadi'ah): Deposit/trust; the property left for safekeeping without liability for loss.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is placing a dirham with a shopkeeper to take goods disliked according to the commentator?
A) Because it involves uncertainty in transactions.
B) Because it is a loan that provides a benefit to the lender, which is prohibited.
C) Because the shopkeeper might cheat.
D) Because it is a form of gambling.

Key Takeaway
Any loan that gives the lender a benefit is prohibited in the Hanafi school, even if it seems like a harmless shopping convenience.

Prohibition of Selling Land in Mecca
Ruling with proofs on prohibition of Meccan land sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling land in Mecca and identify the three proofs (ijma, hadith, and historical practice) cited in the passage.

Core Idea
Mecca's land is a perpetual waqf; selling or renting it is haram by hadith and consensus.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents a clear ruling: selling land in Mecca is absolutely prohibited. The author marshals multiple proofs: an explicit hadith from the Prophet (SAW), the consensus (ijma) of scholars, and the established practice during the time of the Prophet and the early caliphs, where land was treated as common property (sawa'ib). Understanding this ruling helps us appreciate the unique sacredness of Makkah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
The passage opens with a firm ruling: 'it is not permissible to sell its lands now without denial' (لا يجوز بيع أراضيها الآن من غير نكير). This indicates a clear prohibition (haram) reinforced by consensus.
The phrase 'without denial' (من غير نكير) signals that this was the accepted position among scholars.
2
Examine the consensus (ijma)
The author considers the absence of objection as the strongest proof (من أقوى الحجج). This consensus among early scholars adds weight to the ruling.
Ijma is a primary source of law in Hanafi usul.
3
Analyze the hadith proof
The passage quotes the hadith: 'Indeed Allah has made Mecca sacred and prohibited the sale of its lands and their rent.' The verb 'harrama' indicates a definitive prohibition (tahrim).
The hadith covers both sale (bay') and rental (ijarah) of land.
4
Understand the waqf argument
Mecca is described as a waqf of Prophet Ibrahim (al-Khalil). Waqf property cannot be sold; it is perpetually dedicated to public benefit.
This is an additional legal justification, though not a separate proof.
5
Consider historical practice (amal)
During the Prophet's time and the reigns of Abu Bakr and Umar, Meccan lands were treated as common property (sawa'ib). Anyone in need could use them, and when no longer needed, they left them. This shows ownership was not transferred, making sale invalid.
This practice serves as a corroborating evidence (dalil tabi'i).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الكراهية أن لا يجوز بيع أراضيها الآن من غير نكير وهو من أقوى الحجج. وقال الإمام: الإسلام إلى لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام إن الله حرم مكة وحرم بيع أراضيها وإجارتها» ولأنه وقف الخليل عليه الصلاة والسلام ولأن الأراضي بمكة كانت تدعي في زمن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم والخليفتين من بعده بالسوائب من احتاج إليها سكنها ومن استغنى عنها تركها
Book of Disliked Acts: That it is not permissible to sell its lands now without denial, and it is among the strongest proofs. And Imam said: Islam up to his statement, peace be upon him, 'Indeed Allah has made Mecca sacred and prohibited the sale of its lands and their rent.' And because it is a waqf of al-Khalil, peace be upon him, and because the lands in Mecca were claimed in the time of the Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, and the two caliphs after him, as common (sawa'ib): whoever needed it settled in it, and whoever became independent of it left it.
Source pages p. 373

Commentary
This is a ruling with proof regarding the prohibition of selling land in Mecca. The author begins the Book of Disliked Acts with a strong consensus: it is not permissible to sell Meccan land, and there is no known objection—this is among the strongest proofs. The proof includes a hadith that Allah has made Mecca sacred and prohibited selling and renting its lands. Additionally, it is argued that Mecca is a waqf of Prophet Ibrahim (al-Khalil). Historical practice in the time of the Prophet and the first two caliphs supports this: lands were treated as common property (sawa'ib), anyone in need could settle, and when no longer needed they would leave. This shows that ownership was not transferred, thus sale is invalid.

Key Terms
السوائب (al-sawa'ib): Common property, free for anyone to use without ownership transfer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway

Ruling on Depositing Money with a Grocer
Ruling on the disliked nature of depositing money with a grocer under condition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the prohibition of qardh jarr (loan that brings benefit) and apply the ruling to similar conditional deposits in commercial transactions.

Core Idea
A loan that brings benefit to the lender is prohibited.

A common practice is leaving money with a grocer to take goods as needed. However, if this involves a condition that allows the depositor to take goods freely, it becomes problematic because the money effectively becomes a loan to the grocer, and the right to take goods is a benefit for the depositor. This falls under the prohibited category of 'qardh jarr manfa'a' (a loan that brings benefit). The commentator explains the ruling and offers a permissible alternative.

Guided Path
1
Identify the transaction and its condition
Note that the person places a dirham with a grocer and stipulates that he can take whatever goods he wishes. This condition is key.
The condition creates a benefit for the depositor.
2
Analyze the legal nature of the transaction
When the grocer takes the dirham, it is considered a loan (qard) because the depositor allows him to use it. The condition to take goods is a benefit attached to the loan.
This transforms it into a loan that brings benefit (qard jarr).
3
Apply the principle of qardh jarr
The Hanafi school prohibits any loan that brings a benefit to the lender, as it resembles riba. Here, the depositor benefits by having access to goods while the money remains available.
The ruling is makruh tahrimi (prohibitively disliked).
4
Consider the recommended alternative
Instead of making a conditional loan, the depositor should make a deposit (wadi'a) without conditions, then take goods gradually. In that case, the grocer is not liable for loss because it is an amanah.
This avoids the prohibited qardh jarr.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشارح: ومن وضع عند بقال درهماً يأخذ منه ما شاء كره له ذلك لأنه إذا ملكه الدرهم فقد أقرضه إياه وقد شرط أن يأخذ منه من القبول وغيرها ما شاء وله في ذلك نفع بقاء الدرهم وكفايته للحاجات، ولو نفعاً كان في يده لخرج من ساعته ولم يبق فصار في معنى قرض جر وهو منهي عنه، وينبغي يودعه عنده ثم يأخذ منه شيئاً فشيئاً، وإن ضاع فلا شيء عليه لأن الوديعة أمانة ا هـ .
The commentator said: And whoever places a dirham with a grocer to take from him whatever he wishes, it is disliked for him because when he possesses the dirham, he has lent it to him, and he stipulated that he takes from him (the grocer) from the goods and other things whatever he wishes, and he has benefit in that: the remaining of the dirham and its sufficing for needs. If the benefit were in his hand, he would have taken it out at once and it would not remain, so it becomes of the meaning of a loan that brings benefit (qardh jarr), which is prohibited. And it is better for him to deposit it with him, then take from him little by little. And if it is lost, there is no liability on him because the deposit is a trust.
Source pages p. 373

Commentary
This is a ruling statement regarding a specific transaction. The commentator explains that if a person gives a dirham to a grocer with the condition that he can take goods from the grocer freely, this is disliked (makruh) because essentially the dirham becomes a loan to the grocer, and the condition to take goods is a benefit for the lender. This falls under the prohibited category of 'qardh jarr manfa'a' (a loan that brings benefit). The benefit here is that the money remains available for his needs while he gets goods. If the benefit were immediate, he would have taken the money back and bought goods, but by leaving it, he gets both the use of the money and the goods, which is analogous to interest. The recommended alternative is to deposit the money as a trust (wadi'a) and then take goods gradually; if the money is lost, the grocer is not liable because it is an amanah.

Key Terms
قرض جر (qardh jarr): A loan that brings a benefit to the lender; prohibited analogically to riba.
وديعة (wadi'a): A deposit where the depositor leaves property with another for safekeeping without compensation; the depositee is a trustee (amin).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the grocer may misuse the money
B) Because it resembles a loan that brings benefit to the lender
C) Because the dirham is lost permanently
D) Because it is a form of gambling

Key Takeaway
Depositing money with a condition of taking goods freely is prohibited because it is a loan that brings benefit, similar to riba.

Classifying Transactions for Minors in Hanafi Fiqh
Classification of transactions for minors

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify Hanafi transactions for minors into three categories and identify which type of guardian may perform each category.

Core Idea
The Hanafi threefold classification of transactions for minors determines who may legally act for the child.

Islamic law protects minors by restricting who can engage in transactions on their behalf. This passage from Bahr al-Raiq lays out the foundational threefold classification of such transactions: pure benefit, pure harm, and ambiguous. By understanding this classification, you will be able to determine who — from the guardian, uncle, mother, or even the minor himself — may validly perform a given transaction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories of transactions
From the passage, list the three types: naf' mahd (pure benefit), darar mahd (pure harm), and mutaraddid bayn al-naf' wa al-darar (ambiguous). Understand the definition of each.
These are abstract categories; examples are given below.
2
Associate agents with categories
For pure benefit, any caretaker (even the discerning minor himself) may act. For pure harm, no one may act. For ambiguous, only the father, grandfather, or their executors may act.
The mother, uncle, and finder are limited to pure necessities.
3
Analyze the finder's authority using the categories
The finder can only perform pure benefit transactions (e.g., accept gifts) but the text records a dispute: does the finder need the minor to have no father? Trace the disagreement between al-Hidayah and al-Nihayah.
The later view restricts the finder to cases where the father is absent/unknown.
4
Apply the classification to new cases
Given a scenario (e.g., minor inherits a shop, needs to sell it for profit), identify which category applies and who can act.
Selling for profit is ambiguous, so only father/grandfather can do it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (وشراء ما لا بد للصغير منه وبيعه للعم والأم والملتقط لو في حجرهم) يعني بجوز لهؤلاء الثلاثة أن يشتروا للصغير ويبيعوا ما لا بد منه وذلك مثل النفقة والكسوة ولأنه لو لم يكن لهم ذلك لتضرر الصغير وهو ممنوع، وأصله أن التصرفات على الصغير على ثلاثة أقسام : نفع محض فيملكه كل واحد هو في عياله ولياً كان أو أجنبياً كالهبة والصدقة ويملكه الصبي بنفسه إذا كان مميزاً، ونوع هو ضرر محض كالعتاق والطلاق فلا يملكه عليه أحد، ونوع متردد بين النفع والضرر مثل البيع والإجارة للاسترباح فلا يملكه إلا الأب والجد ووصيهما، سواء كان الصغير في أيديهم أو لم يكن لأنهم يتصرفون عليه بحكم الولاية هكذا في الكافي واستئجار الظئر من النوع الأول، وفيه نوع رابع وهو الإنكاح فيجوز لكل عصبة ولذوي الأرحام عند عدم العصبات، وقد تقدم بيان ذلك في كتاب النكاح. قال في الهداية وإنما يجوز للملتقط أن يقبض الهبة للصغير إذا كان لا أب له. قال في النهاية : قوله لا أب له ليس بشرط لازم في حق هذا الحكم لأنه ذكر في كتاب الهبة في صغيرة لها زوج هي عنده يعولها ولها أب فوهب لها جاز ولزوجها أن يقبض الهبة لقيام ولايته عليها بالعول فثبت أن الأب ليس بلازم؛ كذا ذكره فخر الإسلام. وإنما هو قيد اتفاقي ولك أن تقول : إن قول الكل ليس بصحيح إذا الثابت في كتاب الهبة إنما هو ليس بلازم في جواز قبض زوج الصغير الهبة لها إذا كانت عنده يعولها لتفويض الأب ذلك له لا أن عدم الأب ليس بلازم مطلقاً فيما نحن فيه وهو جواز قبض الملتقط الهبة والصدقة لتحقق الفرق بين زوج الصغيرة الذي فوض له الأب أمرها وبين غيره فلا يملكون ذلك إلا بعد موت الأب وقال بعض المتأخرين : المراد بقول صاحب الهداية لا أب له يعني أباً معروفاً وإن كان له أب في قيد الحياة فالحق عندي أن قوله لا أب له قيد احترازي عن اللقيط إذا كان له أب حاضر لا يجوز للملتقط أن يقبض الهبة للصغير ا هـ .
He (may Allah have mercy on him) said: (And purchase of what is necessary for the minor and its sale by the paternal uncle, the mother, and the finder if the minor is under their care) meaning it is permissible for these three to buy for the minor and sell what is necessary, such as food and clothing. For if they did not have that authority, the minor would be harmed, and harm is prevented. The basis is that transactions concerning a minor are of three types: 1) Pure benefit – which is possessed by anyone who has him in his care, whether guardian or stranger, like accepting gifts and charity; the minor himself may possess it if he is discerning. 2) Pure harm – like manumission and divorce; no one may do this on his behalf. 3) Ambiguous between benefit and harm – like buying and selling for profit; only the father, grandfather, and their executors may do this, whether the minor is in their care or not, because they act by virtue of guardianship. This is stated in al-Kafi. Hiring a wet nurse is of the first type. There is a fourth type: marriage – which is permissible for every 'asabah (agnatic relative) and for dhawi al-arham (non-agnatic relatives) in the absence of 'asabah. This has been explained in the Book of Marriage. In al-Hidayah, it states: The finder may accept a gift for the minor only if he has no father. In al-Nihayah: The statement 'he has no father' is not a necessary condition for this ruling because in the Book of Gifts it mentions a minor girl who has a husband, the husband supports her, and she has a father; a gift was given to her, and it was permissible for the husband to accept the gift for her due to his guardianship over her by supporting her. Thus, the father is not a necessary condition. So mentioned Fakhr al-Islam. However, it is a coincidental restriction. But you may say: The statement of 'al-Kull' (everyone?) is not correct because what is established in the Book of Gifts is only that the father is not necessary for the permissibility of the husband accepting the gift for her when she is under his care due to the father delegating authority to him. It does not mean that the absence of father is not necessary absolutely in our topic, which is the permissibility of the finder accepting gifts and charity. There is a difference between the husband of a minor girl to whom the father delegated authority and others; others do not possess that authority except after the death of the father. Some later scholars said: The intended meaning of the author of al-Hidayah's statement 'he has no father' is 'no known father'. Even if he has a living father, in my opinion his statement is a cautionary restriction regarding a foundling: if he has a present father, the finder may not accept a gift for the minor.
Source pages p. 383

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed classification of transactions involving a minor (saghir) in Hanafi law. It explains that the uncle (on father's side), mother, and finder (muaqqi) may only engage in transactions of pure necessity (e.g., buying food and clothing) when the minor is under their care. The underlying principle is that transactions are of three categories: (1) pure benefit (e.g., accepting a gift) – anyone may do this, even the discerning minor himself; (2) pure harm (e.g., divorce, manumission) – no one may do this for the minor; (3) ambiguous (mushtarak between benefit and harm, e.g., buying/selling for profit) – only the father, grandfather, or their executors may do this, as they have valid guardianship (wilayah). The text then discusses the specific case of a finder (luqat) accepting gifts for a foundling, citing a difference between al-Hidayah (requiring no father) and al-Nihayah (not a strict condition, but a contextual one). The final view is that if the foundling has a known living father, the finder cannot accept gifts; if the father is absent or unknown, the finder may act. This shows the nuanced approach to guardianship over foundlings.

Key Terms
نفع محض (naf' mahd): Pure benefit – a transaction that is entirely beneficial to the minor with no potential harm.
ضرر محض (darar mahd): Pure harm – a transaction that is entirely detrimental to the minor (e.g., divorce, manumission).
المتردد بين النفع والضرر (al-mutaraddid bayn al-naf' wa al-darar): Transaction ambiguous between benefit and harm (e.g., buying/selling for profit).
ملتقط (mulaqqit): Finder – a person who picks up a foundling (laqit) and takes responsibility for their care.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi classification in this passage, which transaction is considered 'pure harm' (darar mahd) and thus cannot be performed by any agent on behalf of a minor?
A) Accepting a gift
B) Divorce (talaq)
C) Purchasing food
D) Selling inherited goods for profit

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi threefold classification (pure benefit, pure harm, ambiguous) is the key to determining who may validly transact on a minor's behalf.
