The 'saniyah' is for a camel, used for drawing water from a well. The 'jisr' is a name for what is placed and removed, made of planks and others. The 'qantara' is made of stone? And the 'kuwwah' is an opening in a wall; plural 'kuwan'. If there is a private canal for a man taking from a river between the people, and he wants to build a vault over it or close it from its sides, he may do so because he is disposing of his exclusive property by raising his building. If that increases his taking of water, the partners may prevent him. He may not widen the mouth of the river because it breaks its form and increases his share of water uptake; this is apparent when the division is not by labor, and likewise if it is by labor, because if he widens the mouth, the water subsides at that spot and enters his property more than what he had, or not. Also if he wants to move the mouth back and place it at four cubits from the mouth of the river, because it traps water and increases the water entering it; he does not have that right except with the permission of the partners, unlike if he wants to lower his sluice-gate or raise it in depth at its place, for he may do that in the correct view because the division of water originally took into account the width and narrowness of the sluice-gates, not considering...
Source pages p. 395

Commentary
This passage covers the rulings regarding a shared river among co-owners. The matn states that when co-owners dispute water rights, the water is distributed proportionally to their land holdings, not equally. The commentator compares this to a shared road where co-owners have equal shares regardless of house size, because the purpose of a road is passage, which doesn't vary. For water, the purpose is irrigation, which varies with land area. The text then lists actions that a co-owner cannot unilaterally do: digging a branch canal, installing a watermill, waterwheel, bridge, widening the river mouth, dividing by days if already divided by sluice-gates, or diverting his share to another land without consent. The reasons are that these actions alter the shared property and may harm others. Exceptions exist: if the watermill is placed on his own land (not in the river) and doesn't reduce water, it's allowed. Partners may mutually agree on damming the river for turns. If damming is necessary to get water, they must start with the furthest upstream landowner first. The text also discusses a private canal taking from a shared river: the owner may maintain it without harming others. Widening the mouth is prohibited without consent, but adjusting the depth of one's own sluice-gate is permissible as it doesn't change the original division based on sluice width.

Key Terms
رحى (raha): A watermill; a mill powered by water.
دالية (daliyah): A waterwheel or sweep (long beam with a bucket) used for irrigation.
جسر (jisr): A bridge, often temporary, made of planks.
قنطرة (qantara): A vault or arch, typically made of stone.
كوة (kuwwah (pl. kuwan)): Sluice-gate; an opening in a bank to let water flow.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following actions is permissible without the consent of other co-owners of a shared river?
A) Digging a branch canal to a new field
B) Widening the mouth of the river
C) Lowering the depth of one's own sluice-gate
D) Installing a watermill in the riverbed

Key Takeaway
Unilateral changes to a shared river are prohibited; only actions that do not harm partners or occur on exclusive property are permitted.

Selling Water Rights to Pay Debts: Prohibition and Mechanisms
Ruling on selling water rights without land to pay debts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on selling a water right (shurb) without land and explain the Hanafi method for liquidating such a right to satisfy debts.

Core Idea
A water right cannot be sold separately; debts are paid by attaching it to land or selling the water itself.

When a deceased person leaves only a water right (shurb) and unpaid debts, the Hanafi jurists have provided specific mechanisms to liquidate the asset without violating the principle that shurb is annexed to land. This passage from Al‑Bahr al‑Rāʾiq explains the restrictions and the creative solutions adopted by later Hanafis to honour the debt while respecting the original rule.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
State the original Hanafi narration: shurb cannot be sold without the land it serves.
The text says 'لا يباع الشرب بدون الأرض على رواية الأصل'.
2
Recognize the problem scenario
The deceased dies with debts and owns only a shurb with no land. What can be done?
The shurb cannot be sold alone, so alternative mechanisms are needed.
3
Examine the proposed solutions
Two views are mentioned: (1) collect the water during its turn and sell it, (2) the imam attaches the shurb to another land (with owner consent) and sells the combination.
Both are solutions to a problem, not a general permissibility.
4
Understand the valuation methodology
To determine the shurb's value, it is estimated as if it could be sold, analogous to the 'uqar al-shubha calculation.
This is only for valuation, not for legalizing the sale.
5
Note the fallback option
If no suitable land is found, the imam may buy land from the estate, attach the shurb, and sell the package.
The proceeds first cover the purchased land, then the debt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشارح: لو مات وعليه ديون لا يباع الشرب بدون الأرض على رواية الأصل فإن لم يكن للشرب أرض قيل يجمع الماء في نوبة في حوض فيباع إلى أن يقضي الدين من ذلك، وقيل ينظر لإمام إلى أرض لا شرب لها فيضم هذا الشرب إليها فيبيعها برضا صاحبها، ثم ينظر إلى قيمة الأرض بدون الشرب وإلى قيمتها معه فيصرف تفاوت ما بينهما من الثمن إلى قضاء دين الميت . والسبيل في معرفة قيمة الشرب إذا أراد قسمة الثمن على قيمتهما أن يقوم الشرب على تقدير أن لو كان يجوز بيعه وهو نظير ما قال بعضهم في العقر الواجب بشبهة ينظر إلى هذه المرأة بكم كانت تستأجر للزنا فذلك القدر هو عقدها في الوطء بالشبهة وإن لم يجد اشترى على تركة الميت أرضاً بغير شرب ثم يضم إلى هذا الشرب فيبيعها فيؤدي من الثمن قيمة الأرض المشتراة والفاضل للغرماء .
The commentator said: If someone dies with debts, the water right is not sold without the land according to the original narration. If the water right has no land, it is said: collect the water during its turn in a cistern and sell it until the debt is paid. And it is said: the Imam looks for land without water right, attaches this water right to it, and sells it with the owner's consent. Then he looks at the value of the land without the water right and its value with it, and spends the difference from the price to pay the deceased's debt. The way to know the value of the water right when one wants to divide the price proportionally is to evaluate the water right as if it were permissible to sell it. This is analogous to what some said regarding the dower (ʿuqar) obligatory due to shubha: one looks at how much this woman would be hired for fornication, that amount is her ʿuqar in intercourse with shubha. If he does not find (land without water right), he purchases land without water right from the deceased's estate, then attaches it to this water right, and sells it. From the price, he pays the value of the purchased land, and the surplus is for the creditors.
Source pages p. 398

Commentary
This passage discusses how to liquidate debts when a deceased person owns a water right (shurb) but no land, or when selling water rights is restricted. According to the original narration (riwayat al-asl), a water right cannot be sold separately without land. Two solutions are presented: (1) collect the water during its turn and sell it, or (2) have the imam attach the water right to some land and sell the combination, paying the debt from the added value. The imam must compensate the landowner for any reduction in land value. To determine the water right's standalone value, it is estimated as if it could be sold, analogous to calculating the dower in cases of shubha (where a woman's hire for fornication is used as an analogy—though this analogy is only for valuation methodology, not permissibility). If no suitable land is available, the imam buys land from the estate to attach to the water right, sells the package, and distributes proceeds.

Key Terms
شرب (shurb): A right to a share of water for irrigation, typically attached to a specific land.
عقر (ʿuqar): The dower (mahr) obligatory in cases of vaginal intercourse under shubha (doubtful consent or marriage).
نوبة (nawbah): A scheduled turn for irrigation water distribution.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the original narration, can a water right (shurb) be sold without land to pay debts?
A) Yes, it can be sold separately.
B) No, it cannot be sold without land.
C) Yes, but only with the imam's permission.
D) No, except if the water is collected in a cistern.

Key Takeaway
The original Hanafi rule forbids selling a water right separately from land; debts are satisfied by attaching the right to another land or by selling the water itself, with the imam's oversight.

Defining Khamr: Linguistic Reality and Legal Ruling
Definition of khamr with linguistic and legal disputes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of khamr and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the requirement of foaming.

Core Idea
Khamr is specifically raw grape juice that ferments and foams; other intoxicants are metaphorically called khamr.

The Hanafi school defines khamr precisely as the raw juice of grapes that has fermented and thrown foam. This definition is not merely lexical but has significant legal consequences, affecting rulings such as purity, sale, and criminal penalty. In this lesson, we examine the definition established by Imam Abu Hanifa and the disagreement of the two Sahibayn, as well as the response to those who would generalize the term to all intoxicants.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act under discussion is the consumption of khamr. The matn states that its little and much are prohibited (haram). This establishes the legal focus.
2
Analyze the definition of khamr
The matn defines khamr as the raw juice of grapes that ferments, becomes strong, and throws foam. This is the linguistic and legal definition according to Imam Abu Hanifa.
The term 'khamr' is derived from 'mukhāmara' meaning covering the intellect.
3
Understand the opposing argument
Some scholars (e.g., Shafi'i) argue that every intoxicant is khamr, citing the hadith 'Every intoxicant is khamr.' The Hanafi response is that this hadith is metaphorical, used to convey the ruling (hukm), not the linguistic reality.
The Hanafis rely on the consensus of Arabic linguists that khamr specifically refers to grape juice.
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute
Imam Abu Hanifa requires the juice to throw foam (qadhf bil-zabad) in addition to fermentation (ightila). Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn) consider fermentation sufficient, arguing that the harmful pleasure (ladhdha) is already present.
The thamarah (practical consequence) is that juice that ferments but does not foam is not khamr according to Imam, but is khamr according to Sahibayn.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The matn presents Imam's view as the primary definition, but the commentator notes the disagreement. The school generally relies on the stricter definition, but later fatwa may follow Sahibayn in some contexts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الأشربة قال رحمه الله : (والمحرم منها أربعة الخمر وهي النيء من ماء العنب إذا غلا واشتد وقذف بالزبد وحرم قليلها وكثيرها) وقال بعضهم كل مسكر خمر لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام كل مسكر خمر رواه مسلم. ولقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام الخمر من هاتين النخلة والعنبة رواه مسلم وأبو داود. ولأنها سميت خمر المخامرة العقل وكل مسكر يخامر العقل. ولنا أجماع أهل اللغة على حقيقته في النيء من ماء العنب وتسمية غيرها بالخمر مجازاً وعليه يحمل الحديث المتقدم؛ كذا في الشارح وفيه نظر لأنه نقل في القاموس الخمر ما يسكر من عصير أو عام قال: والعموم أصح. وأيضاً الحديث محمول على بيان الحكم لأنه عليه الصلاة والسلام بعث لبيان الأحكام لا لبيان الحقيقة اللغوية والتعريف المذكور للخمر هو قول الإمام، وعندهما إذا اشتد صار خمراً ولا يشترط فيه القذف بالزبد لأن اللذة تحصل به وهو المؤثر في إيقاع العداوة والصد عن الصلاة وله أن الغليان بداية الشدة وكماله بقذف بالزبد. والكلام فيه في مواضع : أحدهما في بيان ماهيته والثاني وقت ثبوت هذا الاسم وقد تقدما، والثالث أن عينه حرام غير معلول بالسكر بخلاف غيره من الأشربة فإنه معلول بالسكر، ومن الناس من يقول غير المسكر منها ليس بحرام كغيره من الأشربة فإنه معلول بالسكر لأن الفساد لا يحصل إلا به وهذا كفر لأنه مخالف الكتاب والسنة الإجماع. والرابع أنها نجسة العين نجاسة غليظة كالبول والغائط والخامس أن مستحلها يكفر لإنكاره الدليل القطعي . والسادس سقوط تقويمها في حق المسلم حتى لا يضمن متلفها السابع لا يجوز بيعها لقوله صلى الله عليه وسلم إن الذي حرم شربها حرم بيعها رواه مسلم والثامن أنه يحد شاربها وإن لم يسكر. والتاسع الطبخ لا يؤثر فيها لأنه لا يمنع من ثبوت الحرمة لا لرفعها بعد ثبوتها. والعاشر جواز تخليلها على ما يجيء من قريب إن شاء الله تعالى. وفي الكافي : ولا يحل أن يستقيه ذمياً أو صبياً أو دابة وفي الخانية : ويكره الاكتحال بالخمر وأن يجعله في السعوط. وفي الأصل: لو عجن الدقيق بالخمر كره أكله والحنطة إذا وقعت في الخمر يكره أكلها قبل الغسل، ولو انتفخت الحنطة في الخمر قال محمد : لا تطهر قبل الغسل. وقال أبو يوسف : تغسل ثلاث مرات وتجفف في كل مرة فتطهر، وعلى هذا الخلاف إذا طبخ اللحم في الخمر فهو على هذا الخلاف وفي الخلاصة : لو طبخ الخمر بالماء والماء أقل أو سواء يحد شاربه وإن كان الماء أكثر لا يحد إلا ذا سكر. وفي الكافي واختلفوا في سقوط ماليتها والصحيح أنها مال اهـ .
The Book of Drinks. He said (may Allah have mercy on him): 'Among them, four are prohibited: khamr, which is the raw juice of grapes when it ferments, becomes strong, and throws foam; its little and much are prohibited.' Some say every intoxicant is khamr due to his saying (peace be upon him): 'Every intoxicant is khamr' narrated by Muslim. And due to his saying: 'Khamr is from these two: the date palm and the grapevine' narrated by Muslim and Abu Dawud. And because it is called khamr from mukhāmara (covering) the intellect, and every intoxicant covers the intellect. Our proof is the consensus of linguists on its true meaning as the raw juice of grapes, and calling other things khamr is metaphorical, and the previous hadith is interpreted accordingly. Thus in al-Sharih, but there is a critique because in al-Qamus it is said: 'Khamr is what intoxicates from juice or generally' and he said: 'The general meaning is more correct.' Also the hadith is interpreted for clarifying the ruling because he (peace be upon him) was sent to clarify rulings, not linguistic realities. The mentioned definition of khamr is the saying of Imam (Abu Hanifa). According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), when it becomes strong it becomes khamr, and the throwing of foam is not required because pleasure is obtained through it, and it is the effective cause in causing enmity and turning away from prayer. He (the Imam) has that fermentation is the beginning of strength and its completion is by throwing foam. The discussion about it is in several places: first, explaining its essence; second, the time of establishing this name, and both have preceded; third, that its substance is prohibited not conditioned upon intoxication, unlike other drinks whose prohibition is conditioned upon intoxication. Some people say that non-intoxicating ones are not prohibited like other drinks because their prohibition is conditioned upon intoxication since corruption does not occur except through it, and this is disbelief because it contradicts the Book, Sunnah, and consensus. Fourth, that it is impure in substance, severe impurity like urine and feces. Fifth, that whoever deems it permissible becomes a disbeliever for denying a definitive proof. Sixth, the cancellation of its valuation for a Muslim, so the one who destroys it is not liable. Seventh, it is not permissible to sell it due to his saying: 'The one who forbade drinking it forbade selling it' narrated by Muslim. Eighth, the one who drinks it is subject to hadd even if he does not become intoxicated. Ninth, cooking does not affect it because it does not prevent the establishment of prohibition nor lift it after it has been established. Tenth, the permissibility of making it into vinegar, as will come shortly, Allah willing. In al-Kafi: It is not permissible for a dhimmi, a child, or an animal to obtain it. In al-Khaniyya: It is disliked to use khamr as kohl or snuff. In al-Asl: If dough is kneaded with khamr, eating it is disliked. If a grain of wheat falls into khamr, eating it before washing is disliked. If the grain swells in khamr, Muhammad said: It does not become pure before washing. Abu Yusuf said: It should be washed three times and dried each time, then it becomes pure. The same difference applies if meat is cooked in khamr. In al-Khulasa: If khamr is cooked with water and the water is less or equal, the drinker is subject to hadd. If the water is more, he is not subject to hadd unless he becomes intoxicated. In al-Kafi: They differed regarding whether it retains monetary value; the correct view is that it does have value. End.
Source pages p. 400

Commentary
This passage from the beginning of Kitab al-Ashriba defines khamr and explains its severe rulings. The matn from Kanz al-Daqaiq states that among prohibited drinks, khamr is the raw juice of grapes that ferments, becomes strong, and throws foam; both its small and large amounts are forbidden. The commentator (Ibn Nujaym) then discusses the objection that every intoxicant is khamr, using two hadith. The Hanafi response is that linguistically, khamr is specific to grapes, and other intoxicants are called khamr metaphorically. The hadith are interpreted to convey the ruling (hukm shari) not the linguistic definition. The passage then presents the intra-Hanafi dispute: Imam Abu Hanifa requires both fermentation and foaming (qadhf bil-zabad) for juice to become khamr, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad consider fermentation sufficient. The effective cause (illa) according to the Sahibayn is the pleasure (ladhdha) that causes enmity and distracts from prayer; the Imam holds that foaming marks completion. Following this, ten special rulings of khamr are enumerated: (1) its essence (mahiyya), (2) time of naming, (3) its 'ayn is haram unconditionally (not dependent on intoxication), (4) it is najis al-ayn (severe impurity), (5) its permitter becomes a kafir, (6) no monetary value for Muslims (no liability for destruction), (7) sale is prohibited, (8) its drinker is subject to hadd even if not intoxicated, (9) cooking does not change its ruling, (10) making it into vinegar is permissible. Further sub-rulings from other sources are added: it cannot be procured by a dhimmi, it is disliked as kohl or snuff, dough or grains mixed with it are disliked, the difference of Muhammad and Abu Yusuf on purification, and the value debate (the correct view is that it has value).

Key Terms
الخمر (al-khamr): In this text, the raw juice of grapes that has fermented and foamed, becoming intoxicating.
الزبد (al-zabad): The foam/froth on the surface of fermenting grape juice.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the matn, which of the following is part of the definition of khamr?
A) Any intoxicating drink.
B) Juice of grapes that ferments and throws foam.
C) Any drink that covers the intellect.
D) Only wine from dates.

Key Takeaway
Khamr is specifically defined in the Hanafi school as raw grape juice that ferments and foams; other intoxicants are not linguistically khamr but may still be haram.

The Ten Legal Consequences of Khamr
Enumerates ten rulings of khamr with additional furu'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the ten rulings of khamr as presented in Kanz al-Daqaiq and apply each to a practical scenario.

Core Idea
Khamr has ten distinct rulings ranging from impurity to criminal penalty, all rooted in its absolute prohibition.

Once khamr is identified, the law attaches ten specific consequences: it is najis al-ayn, its sale is prohibited, its drinker is flogged even if not drunk, and its permitter becomes a disbeliever. These rulings are unique to khamr and do not apply to other intoxicants in the same strict manner. In this lesson, we go through each ruling and its evidence.

Guided Path
1
List the ten rulings from the text
The passage enumerates ten distinct legal rulings for khamr. Read through the text and extract each one in order.
The rulings start with the essence (mahiyya) and end with the permissibility of making vinegar.
2
Identify the proof for each ruling
Some rulings have explicit textual evidence, such as the hadith prohibiting sale. Others are derived from consensus or reasoning. Note which proofs are cited.
For example, the prohibition of sale is supported by a hadith from Muslim.
3
Apply each ruling to a scenario
Take a practical situation, such as finding a bottle of khamr, and determine how each ruling applies. For example, ruling (6) states that the one who destroys it is not liable.
4
Note sub-rulings from other sources
The passage includes additional details from al-Kafi, al-Khaniyya, and al-Asl. These expand on the ten rulings, such as the difference between Muhammad and Abu Yusuf on purification.
These sub-rulings are authoritative within the Hanafi school.
5
Understand the exception: vinegar making
The tenth ruling allows making khamr into vinegar, which changes its legal status. This is an important exception that prevents waste.
The permissibility of vinegar making will be detailed later in the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الأشربة قال رحمه الله : (والمحرم منها أربعة الخمر وهي النيء من ماء العنب إذا غلا واشتد وقذف بالزبد وحرم قليلها وكثيرها) وقال بعضهم كل مسكر خمر لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام كل مسكر خمر رواه مسلم. ولقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام الخمر من هاتين النخلة والعنبة رواه مسلم وأبو داود. ولأنها سميت خمر المخامرة العقل وكل مسكر يخامر العقل. ولنا أجماع أهل اللغة على حقيقته في النيء من ماء العنب وتسمية غيرها بالخمر مجازاً وعليه يحمل الحديث المتقدم؛ كذا في الشارح وفيه نظر لأنه نقل في القاموس الخمر ما يسكر من عصير أو عام قال: والعموم أصح. وأيضاً الحديث محمول على بيان الحكم لأنه عليه الصلاة والسلام بعث لبيان الأحكام لا لبيان الحقيقة اللغوية والتعريف المذكور للخمر هو قول الإمام، وعندهما إذا اشتد صار خمراً ولا يشترط فيه القذف بالزبد لأن اللذة تحصل به وهو المؤثر في إيقاع العداوة والصد عن الصلاة وله أن الغليان بداية الشدة وكماله بقذف بالزبد. والكلام فيه في مواضع : أحدهما في بيان ماهيته والثاني وقت ثبوت هذا الاسم وقد تقدما، والثالث أن عينه حرام غير معلول بالسكر بخلاف غيره من الأشربة فإنه معلول بالسكر، ومن الناس من يقول غير المسكر منها ليس بحرام كغيره من الأشربة فإنه معلول بالسكر لأن الفساد لا يحصل إلا به وهذا كفر لأنه مخالف الكتاب والسنة الإجماع. والرابع أنها نجسة العين نجاسة غليظة كالبول والغائط والخامس أن مستحلها يكفر لإنكاره الدليل القطعي . والسادس سقوط تقويمها في حق المسلم حتى لا يضمن متلفها السابع لا يجوز بيعها لقوله صلى الله عليه وسلم إن الذي حرم شربها حرم بيعها رواه مسلم والثامن أنه يحد شاربها وإن لم يسكر. والتاسع الطبخ لا يؤثر فيها لأنه لا يمنع من ثبوت الحرمة لا لرفعها بعد ثبوتها. والعاشر جواز تخليلها على ما يجيء من قريب إن شاء الله تعالى. وفي الكافي : ولا يحل أن يستقيه ذمياً أو صبياً أو دابة وفي الخانية : ويكره الاكتحال بالخمر وأن يجعله في السعوط. وفي الأصل: لو عجن الدقيق بالخمر كره أكله والحنطة إذا وقعت في الخمر يكره أكلها قبل الغسل، ولو انتفخت الحنطة في الخمر قال محمد : لا تطهر قبل الغسل. وقال أبو يوسف : تغسل ثلاث مرات وتجفف في كل مرة فتطهر، وعلى هذا الخلاف إذا طبخ اللحم في الخمر فهو على هذا الخلاف وفي الخلاصة : لو طبخ الخمر بالماء والماء أقل أو سواء يحد شاربه وإن كان الماء أكثر لا يحد إلا ذا سكر. وفي الكافي واختلفوا في سقوط ماليتها والصحيح أنها مال اهـ .
The Book of Drinks. He said (may Allah have mercy on him): 'Among them, four are prohibited: khamr, which is the raw juice of grapes when it ferments, becomes strong, and throws foam; its little and much are prohibited.' Some say every intoxicant is khamr due to his saying (peace be upon him): 'Every intoxicant is khamr' narrated by Muslim. And due to his saying: 'Khamr is from these two: the date palm and the grapevine' narrated by Muslim and Abu Dawud. And because it is called khamr from mukhāmara (covering) the intellect, and every intoxicant covers the intellect. Our proof is the consensus of linguists on its true meaning as the raw juice of grapes, and calling other things khamr is metaphorical, and the previous hadith is interpreted accordingly. Thus in al-Sharih, but there is a critique because in al-Qamus it is said: 'Khamr is what intoxicates from juice or generally' and he said: 'The general meaning is more correct.' Also the hadith is interpreted for clarifying the ruling because he (peace be upon him) was sent to clarify rulings, not linguistic realities. The mentioned definition of khamr is the saying of Imam (Abu Hanifa). According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), when it becomes strong it becomes khamr, and the throwing of foam is not required because pleasure is obtained through it, and it is the effective cause in causing enmity and turning away from prayer. He (the Imam) has that fermentation is the beginning of strength and its completion is by throwing foam. The discussion about it is in several places: first, explaining its essence; second, the time of establishing this name, and both have preceded; third, that its substance is prohibited not conditioned upon intoxication, unlike other drinks whose prohibition is conditioned upon intoxication. Some people say that non-intoxicating ones are not prohibited like other drinks because their prohibition is conditioned upon intoxication since corruption does not occur except through it, and this is disbelief because it contradicts the Book, Sunnah, and consensus. Fourth, that it is impure in substance, severe impurity like urine and feces. Fifth, that whoever deems it permissible becomes a disbeliever for denying a definitive proof. Sixth, the cancellation of its valuation for a Muslim, so the one who destroys it is not liable. Seventh, it is not permissible to sell it due to his saying: 'The one who forbade drinking it forbade selling it' narrated by Muslim. Eighth, the one who drinks it is subject to hadd even if he does not become intoxicated. Ninth, cooking does not affect it because it does not prevent the establishment of prohibition nor lift it after it has been established. Tenth, the permissibility of making it into vinegar, as will come shortly, Allah willing. In al-Kafi: It is not permissible for a dhimmi, a child, or an animal to obtain it. In al-Khaniyya: It is disliked to use khamr as kohl or snuff. In al-Asl: If dough is kneaded with khamr, eating it is disliked. If a grain of wheat falls into khamr, eating it before washing is disliked. If the grain swells in khamr, Muhammad said: It does not become pure before washing. Abu Yusuf said: It should be washed three times and dried each time, then it becomes pure. The same difference applies if meat is cooked in khamr. In al-Khulasa: If khamr is cooked with water and the water is less or equal, the drinker is subject to hadd. If the water is more, he is not subject to hadd unless he becomes intoxicated. In al-Kafi: They differed regarding whether it retains monetary value; the correct view is that it does have value. End.
Source pages p. 400

Commentary
This passage from the beginning of Kitab al-Ashriba defines khamr and explains its severe rulings. The matn from Kanz al-Daqaiq states that among prohibited drinks, khamr is the raw juice of grapes that ferments, becomes strong, and throws foam; both its small and large amounts are forbidden. The commentator (Ibn Nujaym) then discusses the objection that every intoxicant is khamr, using two hadith. The Hanafi response is that linguistically, khamr is specific to grapes, and other intoxicants are called khamr metaphorically. The hadith are interpreted to convey the ruling (hukm shari) not the linguistic definition. The passage then presents the intra-Hanafi dispute: Imam Abu Hanifa requires both fermentation and foaming (qadhf bil-zabad) for juice to become khamr, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad consider fermentation sufficient. The effective cause (illa) according to the Sahibayn is the pleasure (ladhdha) that causes enmity and distracts from prayer; the Imam holds that foaming marks completion. Following this, ten special rulings of khamr are enumerated: (1) its essence (mahiyya), (2) time of naming, (3) its 'ayn is haram unconditionally (not dependent on intoxication), (4) it is najis al-ayn (severe impurity), (5) its permitter becomes a kafir, (6) no monetary value for Muslims (no liability for destruction), (7) sale is prohibited, (8) its drinker is subject to hadd even if not intoxicated, (9) cooking does not change its ruling, (10) making it into vinegar is permissible. Further sub-rulings from other sources are added: it cannot be procured by a dhimmi, it is disliked as kohl or snuff, dough or grains mixed with it are disliked, the difference of Muhammad and Abu Yusuf on purification, and the value debate (the correct view is that it has value).

Key Terms
الخمر (al-khamr): In this text, the raw juice of grapes that has fermented and foamed, becoming intoxicating.
الزبد (al-zabad): The foam/froth on the surface of fermenting grape juice.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is true about cooking khamr?
A) It becomes permissible if cooked thoroughly.
B) It is still unlawful and the drinker may be subject to hadd.
C) It becomes pure after cooking.
D) Cooking removes the prohibition entirely.

Key Takeaway
Khamr carries ten specific legal rulings that cover purity, liability, sale, consumption, and punishment; these are unique and severe.

Definition and Ruling of Tila'
Defines tila' and discusses its legal status, impurity, and related drinks.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of tila', explain its legal status as haram with light impurity, and identify the mufta bihi regarding liability for its destruction.

Core Idea
Tila' is haram but its impurity is light, and the seller is not liable for its destruction.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq defines a specific category of cooked grape juice known as tila'. The author clarifies the correct definition, the dispute over its impurity, and the ruling on its sale and liability. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for correctly applying Hanafi fiqh in transactions involving intoxicating drinks.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of tila'
Determine the exact proportion of evaporation that defines tila' according to the passage. Note that the matn says 'less than two-thirds' but the sharh corrects it to 'two-thirds gone, one-third remains.'
2
Determine the ruling on its impurity
Note the two views on its impurity (severe vs. light) and identify which is the relied-upon (zahir al-riwaya) position.
3
Analyze the dispute on liability
Compare the positions of Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) regarding compensation if the drink is destroyed.
4
Apply the fatwa hierarchy
Identify which position is mufta bihi (operative for fatwa) and understand the reasoning from al-Zahiriyya.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (والطلاء وهو العصير إن طبخ حتى ذهب أقل من ثلثيه) وهذا النوع ماء العنب حتى ذهب ثلثاه حتى الثالث : للمثلث الثاني. قال في المحيط الطلاء اسم ما طبخ من وهو وبقي ثلثه وصار مسكراً وهو الصواب، وإنما سمي طلاء لقول عمر ما أشبه هذا بطلاء البعير وهو النفط الذي يطلى به البعير إذا كان أجرب . ونجاسته قيل مغلظة، وقيل مخففة وهو ظاهر الرواية. وإن طبخ حتى ذهب أكثر من نصفه فحكمه حكم الباذق والمنصف في ظاهر الرواية. وفي الظهيرية : ويجوز بيع الباذق والمنصف والمسكر ونقيع الزبيب ويضمن متلفهم في قول الإمام خلافاً لهما والفتوى على قولهما .اهـ. وفي الينابيع : الطلاء ما يطبخ من عصير العنب في نار أو شمس حتى ذهب ثلثاه وبقي ثلثه وهو عصير محض، فإن كان فيه شيء من ذهب ثلثاه بقي المجموع من الماء والعصير .اهـ. وفي الهداية : ويسمى الطلاء الباذق أيضاً سواء كان الذاهب قليلاً أو كثيراً، والمنصف ما ذهب نصفه وبقي نصفه وكل ذلك حرام اهـ. وعندنا إذا غلا واشتد بالزبد وإذا اشتد ولم يقذف بالزبد فهو على الخلاف بين الإمام وصاحبيه كما تقدم .
He said (may Allah have mercy on him): '(And al-tila': it is juice that is cooked until less than two-thirds of it goes away.) This is the type of grape juice until two-thirds go away, until the third: for the second tripled? It is said in al-Muhit: al-tila' is the name of what is cooked from, and its third remains and it becomes intoxicating, and that is correct. It is called tila' because of the saying of 'Umar: 'This resembles the tila' of a camel,' which is the tar used to coat a mangy camel. Its impurity: some say severe, some say light, and the light is the zahir al-riwaya. If it is cooked until more than half goes away, its ruling is the ruling of al-badhq and al-munsif in the zahir al-riwaya. In al-Zahiriyya: It is permissible to sell al-badhq, al-munsif, al-sakar, and naqi' al-zabib, and the one who destroys them is liable according to the saying of Imam, contrary to the two, and the fatwa is on their saying. End. In al-Yanabi': al-tila' is what is cooked from grape juice by fire or sun until two-thirds go away and one-third remains, and it is pure juice. If there is something in it of which two-thirds have gone, the remaining mixture is of water and juice. End. In al-Hidaya: Al-tila' is also called al-badhq, whether the amount gone is little or much, and al-munsif is that of which half has gone and half remains, and all of that is haram. End. According to us, if it ferments and becomes strong with foam, and if it becomes strong but does not throw foam, then it is subject to the dispute between Imam and his two companions as previously mentioned.
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This passage defines al-tila' (cooked grape juice) and its legal rulings. The matn states that it is juice cooked until less than two-thirds evaporates, but the sharh corrects that it is until two-thirds evaporate (i.e., one-third remains). The name 'tila' is derived from 'Umar's analogy to camel tar. The key point is that its impurity is disputed: some say severe, but the reliable view (zahir al-riwaya) is that it is light (mukhaffafa). The status of related drinks: if cooked more than half, it is like al-badhq and al-munsif, which are also haram according to al-Hidaya. However, in al-Zahiriyya, selling these is deemed permissible (a minority view) and the liability for destruction follows the Sahibayn (no liability) as the fatwa. The passage ends by noting that the foam dispute applies here as well. Thus, tila' is haram but less severe than khamr; its najasa is light; and the mufta bihi is that the seller is not liable for damages.

Key Terms
الطلاء (al-tila'): Grape juice cooked until two-thirds evaporates and one-third remains.
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwaya): The well-known transmitted ruling from the Hanafi school, here referring to the impurity of tila' being light.
الباذق (al-badhq): Cooked grape juice; sometimes synonymous with tila'.
المنصف (al-munsif): Cooked grape juice where half has evaporated and half remains.
السكر (al-sakar): An intoxicating drink, possibly date wine.
نقيع الزبيب (naqi' al-zabib): Raisin infusion that becomes intoxicating.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the relied-upon ruling (mufta bihi) regarding the sale of tila' and related drinks?
A) Sale is prohibited and the seller is liable for damages
B) Sale is permitted but the seller is liable for damages
C) Sale is permitted and the seller is not liable for damages
D) Sale is prohibited but the seller is not liable for damages

Key Takeaway
Tila' (cooked grape juice with one-third remaining) is haram, its impurity is light, and according to the fatwa, its sale is permissible with no liability for destruction.

Sakar and Naqi' al-Zabib: Definitions and Lesser Prohibition
Defines sakar and naqi' al-zabib, discusses their legal status and the kufr objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definitions of sakar and naqi' al-zabib, explain their lesser prohibition, and identify the ruling on the denier of their prohibition.

Core Idea
Sakar and naqi' al-zabib are prohibited but with lighter consequences: no hadd, no takfir for the denier, and light impurity.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq defines two key types of prohibited drinks: sakar (raw date juice) and naqi' al-zabib (raisin infusion). It clarifies that their prohibition is less severe than khamr, and addresses a critical objection: if the prohibition is established by consensus (ijma), why is the denier not considered a disbeliever? Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly applying Hanafi rulings on intoxicants.

Guided Path
1
Identify the drinks defined
Locate the definitions of sakar (raw date juice) and naqi' al-zabib (raisin infusion). Note that sakar is prohibited only after it throws foam; fadikh is also mentioned.
Sakar is from fresh dates; naqi' al-zabib from raisins.
2
Understand the conditions for prohibition
For sakar and fadikh, the condition is that the drink ferments and throws foam. For naqi' al-zabib, it becomes strong (shadd). Before these conditions, the drinks are halal.
Contrast with khamr which is prohibited as soon as it ferments.
3
Analyze the verse argument and Hanafi response
Shuraik used Q.16:67 to argue permissibility. Hanafis respond that the verse refers to before prohibition or is reproachful. This shows how conflicting proofs are reconciled.
The verse is not a clear proof for permissibility after the general prohibition of intoxicants.
4
Understand the overall ruling (lesser prohibition)
These drinks are haram but with lighter consequences: no hadd for drinking, light impurity, and the one who destroys them is liable (Imam's view). Abu Yusuf allows sale if cooked more than half.
The lighter status is specific to these drinks, not khamr.
5
Evaluate the takfir objection and Hanafi response
An objector argues that ijma is qat'i, so deniers become kafir. Hanafis respond that the specific ijma on sakar is transmitted via ahad, not mutawatir, so it lacks certainty.
This is a key usul al-fiqh point: not all ijma is equally definitive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (والسكر وهو النيء من ماء الرطب) وهذا هو النوع الأشربة المحرمة مشتق من سكرت الريح إذا سكنت وإنما يحرم إذا قذفت بالزبد من وقبله حلال. وقال شريك بن عبد الله . وهو حلال وإذا قذف بالزبد لقوله تعالى تتخذون سكراً ورزقاً حسناً [النحل: ٦٧] امتن علينا به والامتنان لا يكون بالمحرم ولنا ما روينا، والآية محمولة على الابتداء حين كانت الأشربة مباحة، وقيل أريد بها التوبيخ ومعناها والله أعلم تتخذون منه سكراً وتدعونه رزقاً حسناً. والثاني الفضيخ وهو النيء من البسر المذنب إذا غلا واشتد وقذف بالزبد فإنه اسم مشتق من الفضخ وهو الكسر، يقال انفضخ سنام البعير أي انكسر من الحمل فلما كان البسر ينكسر لاستخراج المستخرج بعد الفضخ ؛ كذا في المحيط. قال رحمه الله : ( ونقيع الزبيب وهو النيء من ماء الزبيب) وهو الرابع من الأشرية المحرمة إذا اشتد لما قدمنا ثم حرمة هذه الأشياء دون حرمة لا يكفر مستحلها ولا يجب الحد بشربها ونجاستها خفيفة ويضمن متلفها عند الإمام على ما بينا في الغصب وعن أبي يوسف يجوز بيعها إذا كان الذاهب بالطبخ أكثر من النصف. ولقائل أن يقول : من هذه الأشربة نقيع التمر وهو السكر وقد استدللنا على حرمته بإجماع الصحابة وقد تقرر أن الإجماع دليل قطعي فيكفر مستحلها فيكيف قلتم لا يكفر مستحلها؟ ويجاب بأنه قد يكون نقل الإجماع بطريق الآحاد فلا يفيد القطع والمنقول في حرمة السكر من هذا القبيل. وفي المحيط ونقيع الزبيب نوعان وهو أن ينقع الزبيب في الماء حتى منه الخمر حتى : الماء منه سمي الماء البحر الرائق / ج ٨/ ٢٦٥
He said (may Allah have mercy on him): '(And al-sakar: it is the raw juice of fresh dates.)' This is the type of prohibited drinks, derived from 'the wind calmed down' when it settles. It is only prohibited when it throws foam; before that it is halal. Shuraik ibn 'Abdullah said it is halal even when it throws foam, relying on the verse: 'You take from it al-sakar and goodly provision' (al-Nahl 16:67), as a gratitude to us, and gratitude cannot be for something prohibited. Our proof is what we have narrated, and the verse is interpreted as referring to the initial period when drinks were permissible, or it is meant as reproach, its meaning being: 'You take from it sakar and leave goodly provision.' The second is al-fadikh: the raw juice of the ripening dates (busr) when it ferments, becomes strong, and throws foam. It is derived from 'fadkh' meaning break, as the camel's hump breaks from a load; similarly, the date splits to extract what is inside. Thus in al-Muhit. He said (may Allah have mercy on him): '(And naqi' al-zabib: it is the raw juice of raisins.)' This is the fourth of the prohibited drinks when it becomes strong, as previously mentioned. Then the prohibition of these things is lesser: their permitter is not a disbeliever, their drinker is not subject to hadd, their impurity is light, and the one who destroys them is liable according to Imam as we clarified in ghasb. From Abu Yusuf: it is permissible to sell them if the amount gone by cooking is more than half. Someone might object: Among these drinks is naqi' al-tamr (date infusion), which is sakar, and we have proven its prohibition by consensus of the Companions, and consensus is a definitive proof, so its permitter becomes a disbeliever. How can you say he does not become a disbeliever? The answer is that the transmission of that consensus may be through ahad (isolated) channels, so it does not give certainty, and the transmitted consensus on sakar's prohibition is of this type. In al-Muhit: naqi' al-zabib is of two types: that raisins are soaked in water until from it 'khamr'? ... (corrupted).
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This passage defines two more prohibited drinks: sakar (raw date juice) and naqi' al-zabib (raisin infusion). For sakar, it is only prohibited after foaming; before that it is halal. The verse of Qur'an (al-Nahl 67) is cited by Shuraik as proof of permissibility, but the Hanafi response is that the verse refers to the time before prohibition, or it is a reproach. The second drink is al-fadikh (from ripening dates). The fourth is raisin infusion. The overall ruling for these three (sakar, fadikh, naqi' al-zabib) is that they are prohibited but less severely: their permitter does not become a kafir, no hadd for drinking, light impurity. On liability, Imam holds they have value, but Abu Yusuf allows selling them when cooked more than half (i.e., when they become non-intoxicating? Actually, the text says 'if the amount gone by cooking is more than half' which might mean it becomes non-intoxicating). An important objection is raised: if sakar is proven haram by ijma of the Companions, which is a definitive proof, then its permitter should be a kafir. The answer is that the transmission of that ijma is not mutawatir (mass-transmitted) but ahad (single-chain), so it does not yield certainty. Thus, rejection does not amount to kufr.

Key Terms
السكر (al-sakar): The raw juice of fresh dates that has fermented and thrown foam.
نقيع الزبيب (naqi' al-zabib): The raw juice of raisins (soaked in water) that has become strong (fermented).
الإجماع (al-ijma'): Consensus of the Companions on the prohibition of sakar, but transmitted through ahad chains.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why, according to the passage, does the one who deems sakar (date wine) permissible NOT become a disbeliever?
A) Because sakar is not mentioned in the Quran
B) Because the consensus on its prohibition is not definitively transmitted
C) Because sakar is actually halal according to some scholars
D) Because drinking sakar is only makruh, not haram

Key Takeaway
Sakar and naqi' al-zabib are prohibited but with lighter sanctions: no hadd for drinking, no takfir for the denier, because the evidence (ijma) is not definitively transmitted.

The Permissibility of Nabidh in Special Vessels
Ruling with proof on the permissibility of nabidh in four vessels, with abrogation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on making nabidh in dubba', hantam, muzafat, and naqir, and identify the hadith proof and its abrogation.

Core Idea
Containers do not make a drink permissible or prohibited; only intoxication matters.

This lesson examines the well-known hadith in which the Prophet (صلى الله عليه وسلم) initially forbade making nabidh in four specific vessels, then later abrogated that prohibition. The Hanafi school concludes that the vessels themselves are irrelevant; the only criterion is whether the drink becomes intoxicating. We will also define each vessel and clarify a common misunderstanding about their status.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling in the Matn
Read the opening line of the passage: 'وحل الانتباذ في الدباء والحنتم والمزفت والنقير'. The author states that making nabidh in these four vessels is permissible (halal).
The ruling is from the later abrogated permission, not the initial prohibition.
2
Locate the Dalil (Proof)
The author cites the hadith: 'كنت نهيتكم عن الأشربة في ظروف إلا فاشربوا في كل وعاء غير أنكم لا تشربوا مسكراً'. This hadith is reported by Muslim, Ahmad, and others.
The hadith explicitly abrogates the earlier prohibition.
3
Analyse the Wajh al-Dalalah (How the Proof Indicates the Ruling)
The phrase 'فاشربوا في كل وعاء' gives general permission to use any container, and 'غير أنكم لا تشربوا مسكراً' restricts only to avoiding intoxicants. Thus, the container is irrelevant; only the drink's intoxicating nature matters.
This is a clear dalala (meaning) from the text.
4
Understand the Abrogation (Naskh)
The Prophet (صلى الله عليه وسلم) initially forbade these specific vessels to block a means to intoxication, but later permitted them when the community understood that intoxication alone is the prohibition. The vessels are not inherently impure or prohibited.
The abrogation is established by this authentic hadith.
5
Note the Subsidiary Purification Issue
The passage continues with the condition that if the vessels were previously used for wine, they must be washed. This will be covered in the next lesson.
For new vessels that never held wine, no washing is needed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الانتباذ في الدباء والحنتم والمزفت والنقير وخل الخمر سواء خللت أو تخللت وكره لو طبخ فيه بعد العصير. لو جمع بين العنب والتمر أو بين العنب والزبيب فطبخ لا يحل حتى يذهب ثلثاه لأن التمر والزبيب وإن كان يكتفي فيه بأدنى طبخه فعصير العنب لا بد أن يذهب ثلثاه فيعتبر جانب العنب احتياطاً للحرمة. وكذا إذا جمع بين عصير العنب ونقيع التمر لما قلنا، ولو طبخ نقيع التمر أو نقيع الزبيب أدنى طبخة ثم نقع فيه تمراً أو زبيباً إن كان ما مثله لا نقع فيه شيئاً ما يسيراً لا يتخذ النبيذ من مثله فلا بأس به، وإن كان يتخذ النبيذ من يحل كما إذا صب في المطبوخ قدح من نقيع والمعنى تغليب جهة الحرمة ولا حد في شربه لأن التحريم للاحتياط والاحتياط في الحد في دريئه ولو طبخ الخمر أو غيره بعد الاشتداد حتى ذهب ثلثاه لم يحل لأن الحرمة قد تقرر فلا ترتفع بالطبخ. وفي الظهيرية : الفضيخ الشراب المتخذ من التمر فإذا أفضخ التمر وقذف ثم ينقع في الماء حتى تخرج حلاوته ثم يترك حتى يشتد فإذا اشتد حرم. وفي التهذيب عن الثاني والثالث : البسر المذنب إذا طبخ أدنى طبخة فإذا حلي يحل شربه بلا خلاف فإذا اشتد فحكمه كالمثلث. وفي الجامع : السكران الذي يحد هو الذي لا يعقل مطلقاً قليلاً كان أو كثيراً ولا يعرف الرجل من المرأة ولا الأرض من السماء عند الإمام وفي شربه الأصل إذا ذهب عقله وكان كلامه مخبطاً يعتبر الغالب، وإن كان النصف مستقيماً والنصف غير مستقيم لا يقام عليه الحد. وفي القدوري : إذا غلب عليه زال طعمها وريحها فلا حد في شربها. وفيه أيضاً عن الثاني : إذا بل في الخمر خبزاً فأكل الخبز إذا كان الطعم يوجد حد ، وإن كان لا يرى أثرها في الخبز لا، وإذا شرب الخمر لضرورة مخافة العطش فشرب مقدار ما يرويه فسكر فلا حد وإن ادعى الإكراه لم يصدق لأن الإكراه لا يتحقق إلا بالبينة .اهـ. تصرفات السكران كلها نافذة إلا الردة والإقرار بالحدود الخالصة .اهـ. قال رحمه الله : وحل الانتباذ في الدباء والحنتم والمزفت والنقير) لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام) كنت نهيتكم عن الأشربة في ظروف إلا فاشربوا في كل وعاء غير أنكم لا تشربوا مسكراً» (۱) رواه مسلم وأحمد وغيرهما. ولأن الظرف لا يحل حراماً ولا يحرم والدباء هو القرع والنقير هو أصل النخلة ينقر نقراً وينسج نسجاً، والمزفت وهو النقير، والحنتم الجرار الخضر . قيل الحنتم الجرار الحمر. ثم إن انتبذ في هذه الأوعية قبل استعمالها في الخمر فلا إشكال في حله وطهارته وإن استعمل فيها الخمر ثم انتبذ فيها ينظر؛ إن كان الوعاء عتيقاً يطهر بغسله ثلاث مرات وإن كان جديداً لا يظهر عند محمد، وعند أبي يوسف يغسل ثلاثاً ويجفف في كل مرة بعد مرة أخرى حتى إذا خرج الماء صافياً غير متغير لنا أو طعاماً أو ريحاً حكم بطهارته .اهـ.
Making nabidh in gourds, green jars, pitch-coated vessels, and hollowed palm trunks, and vinegar of wine whether it turns to vinegar by itself or by artificial means, and it is disliked if one cooks [grape juice] in it after pressing. If one mixes grapes and dates or grapes and raisins and cooks [the mixture], it is not permissible until two-thirds of it have evaporated, because for dates and raisins, a minimal cooking is sufficient, but grape juice requires that two-thirds disappear, so the grape side is taken into account as a precaution for prohibition. Similarly, if one mixes grape juice and date infusion, as we said. If one cooks date infusion or raisin infusion with a minimal cooking, then soaks dates or raisins in it, if the like of that is not soaked in it—something insignificant from which nabidh is not made—then there is no harm; but if nabidh is made from it, it becomes permissible, as when a cup of infusion is poured into the cooked product. The rationale is giving weight to the aspect of prohibition. There is no fixed penalty for drinking it because the prohibition is precautionary, and precaution in penalty is to drop it. If one cooked wine or other [intoxicant] after it became strong until two-thirds disappeared, it does not become permissible because the prohibition has been established and is not removed by cooking. In al-Zahiriyyah: al-Fadikh is a drink made from dates; when dates are crushed and thrown, then soaked in water until their sweetness comes out, then left until it becomes strong, when it becomes strong it becomes prohibited. In al-Tahdhib from the second and third: unripe dates with stalks, when cooked minimally, and if they become sweet, drinking it is permissible without disagreement; when it becomes strong, its ruling is like that of the three-times-reduced drink. In al-Jami': the intoxicated person who is subject to the prescribed punishment is the one who has no intellect at all, little or much, and does not distinguish between a man and a woman or between earth and sky according to Imam Abu Hanifa. Regarding drinking, the principle is: if his intellect leaves and his speech is incoherent, the predominant [condition] is considered; if half is coherent and half incoherent, the prescribed punishment is not imposed on him. In al-Quduri: if it overcomes him and its taste and smell disappear, then there is no prescribed punishment for drinking it. Also in it from the second: if one soaks bread in wine and eats the bread, if the taste [of wine] is found, punishment is imposed; if no trace of it is seen in the bread, then no. If one drinks wine out of necessity due to fear of thirst, drinking enough to quench his thirst, and becomes intoxicated, there is no punishment; if he claims compulsion, he is not believed because compulsion is only established by evidence. The actions of an intoxicated person are all effective except apostasy and confession of pure prescribed punishments. He (the author) said, may Allah have mercy on him: The permissibility of making nabidh in gourds, green jars, pitch-coated vessels, and hollowed palm trunks, due to his saying, peace be upon him: 'I had forbidden you from drinks in certain containers, but now drink in every container, as long as you do not drink an intoxicant'—reported by Muslim, Ahmad, and others. And because the container does not make permissible what is impermissible nor does it make forbidden. Al-Dubba' is gourd; al-Naqir is the base of a palm tree, hollowed out and woven; al-Muzafat is the pitch-coated one; al-Hantam is green jars—some say red jars. Then, if one makes nabidh in these vessels before they have been used for wine, there is no doubt about its permissibility and purity. If they have been used for wine and then one makes nabidh in them, consider: if the vessel is old, it becomes pure by washing it three times; if it is new, it does not become pure according to Muhammad. According to Abu Yusuf, it is washed three times and dried after each time until the water comes out clear, unchanged in color, taste, or smell, then it is judged pure.
Source pages p. 403

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof on the permissibility of making nabidh in four specific types of vessels: gourds, green jars, pitch-coated vessels, and hollowed palm trunks. The author begins by stating the ruling of the matn: making nabidh in these vessels is permissible, and likewise vinegar from wine is permissible whether it turns by itself or by artificial means. Then the commentary expands on this by quoting a Hadith in which the Prophet (صلى الله عليه وسلم) originally forbade these containers but later abrogated that prohibition, allowing any container as long as the drink is not intoxicating. The commentary explains the names of the vessels: dubba' is gourd, naqir is a hollowed palm trunk, muzafat is pitch-coated, hantam are green (or red) jars. Then it addresses a nuance: if these vessels have been used for wine before, they need to be washed. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Muhammad and Imam Abu Yusuf regarding new vessels previously used for wine. Abu Yusuf requires washing three times with drying in between until water runs clear; Muhammad holds that new vessels cannot be purified by washing because the wine has penetrated. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that of Abu Yusuf.

Key Terms
الانتباذ (al-intibadh): Making nabidh, a non-intoxicating fermented drink from grapes, dates, etc.
دباء (dubba'): Gourd used as a container for liquids.
حنتم (hantam): Green (or red) earthenware jars.
مزفت (muzafat): Vessel coated with pitch (tar) to seal it.
نقير (naqir): Hollowed-out palm trunk used as a container.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is the correct evidence for the permissibility of making nabidh in the four vessels?
A) Analogy to other permissible drinks.
B) The hadith 'كنت نهيتكم عن الأشربة في ظروف إلا فاشربوا في كل وعاء غير أنكم لا تشربوا مسكراً'.
C) Consensus of the Companions that the vessels are permissible.
D) The verse 'يَا أَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا إِنَّمَا الْخَمْرُ وَالْمَيْسِرُ...'.

Key Takeaway
The four vessels are permissible for nabidh making based on the abrogated hadith; the only condition is that the drink does not become intoxicating.

Purification of Vessels Previously Used for Wine
Intra-Hanafi dispute on purification of vessels previously used for wine

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate the intra-Hanafi opinions of Imam Muhammad and Abu Yusuf on washing new vessels previously used for wine, and state the mufta bihi.

Core Idea
New vessels that absorbed wine cannot be purified according to Muhammad; Abu Yusuf requires three washes with drying, and this is the relied-upon position.

The previous lesson established that the four vessels are permissible for nabidh. But a practical question arises: what if these vessels had been used for wine before? The Hanafi school has an internal disagreement between Imam Muhammad and Imam Abu Yusuf regarding new vessels. This lesson unpacks their respective arguments and identifies the operative ruling for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
State the Issue: Vessel Used for Wine
The passage distinguishes between old and new vessels. For old vessels (that have been used for wine multiple times?), there is agreement that washing three times purifies. For new vessels, there is a dispute.
The text says: 'إن كان الوعاء عتيقاً يطهر بغسله ثلاث مرات وإن كان جديداً لا يظهر عند محمد'.
2
Imam Muhammad's Position
Muhammad holds that a new vessel previously used for wine cannot be purified by washing. His reasoning likely stems from the belief that the najasah (wine) penetrates deeply into the material and cannot be removed by mere washing.
This view is stricter.
3
Imam Abu Yusuf's Position
Abu Yusuf holds that three washes with drying in between purifies the vessel. The criterion is that the water comes out clear, unchanged in color, taste, or smell.
This view is more practical and allows reuse.
4
Determine the Mufta Bihi (Operative Ruling)
The relied-upon position for fatwa is that of Abu Yusuf, as it is more precautionary (in terms of allowing purification) and is consistent with the general principle that washing removes najasah.
The author of al-Bahr al-Raiq likely supports this.
5
Practical Application
If you have a new clay pot that had wine, you should wash it three times, drying after each wash, and if the water runs clear, it is pure and may be used for nabidh.
If the pot is old, a single triple wash suffices even without drying.

Key Terms
الانتباذ (al-intibadh): Making nabidh, a non-intoxicating fermented drink from grapes, dates, etc.
غسل (ghusl): Washing, specifically the act of purifying a vessel by washing it with water.
تجفيف (tajfif): Drying, a step required by Abu Yusuf between washes for new vessels.
عتيق (atiq): Old vessel; one that has been used for wine previously and has already absorbed some wine.
جديد (jadid): New vessel; one that has never been used for wine (but in this context, it has been used for wine once and is considered new in terms of absorption).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi (relied-upon opinion), how should a new vessel that held wine be purified?
A) It cannot be purified; discard it.
B) Wash it three times with drying in between, then it is pure.
C) Wash it once without drying.
D) Leave it unused for a year.

Key Takeaway
For new vessels used for wine, the relied-upon Hanafi view is that washing three times with drying in between purifies the vessel.

Vinegar from Wine: Permissibility and Transformation (Istihalah)
Ruling on vinegar from wine with proofs and subsidiary issues

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on vinegar produced from wine, identify the dalil (hadith and qiyas), and distinguish the Hanafi position from the Shafi'i.

Core Idea
Wine becomes pure vinegar by istihalah — transformation makes it halal.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses the permissibility of vinegar made from wine, a point of dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools. The Hanafi school holds that the transformation (istihalah) purifies the wine regardless of how it occurs, based on a general hadith and analogy to slaughtered animals. The Shafi'i school restricts permissibility to natural transformation only. Let's examine the proofs and reasoning.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
What exactly does the Hanafi school rule on vinegar from wine? Note that it applies whether transformation is natural or artificial.
The ruling includes permissibility of consumption and purity of the vinegar and its container.
2
Identify the Shafi'i counter-position
What is the opposing view? When does the Shafi'i school consider the vinegar impure?
The disagreement is specifically on artificial transformation (e.g., adding salt, vinegar, or moving to sun).
3
Analyze the Hanafi proofs
Examine the two proofs: the hadith 'نعم الإدام الخل' (general) and the qiyas (analogy) with slaughtered animals. Understand the illa (effective cause) of istihalah.
The hadith is general (amm) and includes all forms of vinegar. The qiyas shows that transformation from corrupt to wholesome purifies.
4
Apply the ruling to subsidiary issues
Consider the purity of the container, the case of bitter vinegar, and the effect of cooking or kneading with wine.
The correct view is that the container becomes pure; bitter vinegar is subject to disagreement among the three Hanafi imams.

وخل الخمر سواء خللت أو تخللت ذلك خل الخمر فلا فرق في بين أن يتخلل بنفسه أو تخلل بإلقاء شيء فيه كالملح أو الخل أو النقل من الظل إلى الشمس أو بإيقاد النار بالقرب منها خلافاً للشافعي إذا تخللت بالقاء شيء فيها كالملح ولنا قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام (نعم) إلا دم الخل» (۲) مطلقاً فيتناول جميع صورها ولأن بالتخليل إزالة الوصف المفسد وثبات صفة الصلاح كالذبائح فالتخليل أولى لما فيه من إحراز مال يصير جلالاً ثم فعل ذلك غير حكمه من الحرمة إلى الحل ومن النجاسة إلى الطهارة ألا ترى أن ظرفها كان طاهراً تنجس بها فإذا طهر بالتخليل طهر جميع أجزائه وأجزاء إنائه هو الصحيح. وقيل : لا يطهر لأنه تنجس بإهانة الخمر ولم يوجد ما يوجب طهارته فيبقى على ما كان ولو غسل بالخل فتخلل من ساعته طهر للاستحالة، وكذا إذا صب منه الخمر ثم مليء خلاً يطهر في الحال. وفي المحيط : ولو كان الخل فيه حموضة غالبة وطعم المرارة فإنه لا يحل ما لم تزل من كل وجه وعندهما يحل واعتبر الغالب منها. ولو صب في المرقة خمر فطبخ لم يحل لأنه تنجس قبل الطبخ فلا يحل بالطبخ ولو عجن الدقيق بالخمر صار نجسا.
And vinegar of wine, whether it becomes vinegar by itself or by artificial means, that is vinegar of wine; there is no difference between it becoming vinegar by itself or by throwing something into it like salt, or vinegar, or transferring from shade to sun, or by lighting fire near it, contrary to al-Shafi'i when it becomes vinegar by throwing something into it like salt. Our evidence is his statement, peace be upon him: 'What an excellent condiment is vinegar' (Sunan Abu Dawud, etc.)—absolutely, so it includes all forms. And because by turning into vinegar, the corruptive quality is removed and the quality of wholesomeness is established, like slaughtered animals; so turning into vinegar is more appropriate because it secures property that becomes good. Then that action changed its ruling from prohibition to permissibility and from impurity to purity. Do you not see that its container was pure, then became impure by it, so when it becomes pure by the turning, all its parts and the parts of its vessel become pure—this is correct. And it is said: it does not become pure because it became impure by the disrespect of wine, and nothing occurred that necessitates its purity, so it remains as it was. If one washes it with vinegar and it turns immediately, it becomes pure due to transformation. Similarly, if one pours out the wine from it and then fills it with vinegar, it becomes pure immediately. In al-Muhit: If the vinegar has predominant acidity and a bitter taste, it is not permissible until that is removed entirely; according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), it is permissible and the predominant is considered. If one pours wine into broth and cooks it, it does not become permissible because it became impure before cooking, so cooking does not make it permissible. And if one kneads flour with wine, it becomes impure.
Source pages p. 403

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the permissibility of vinegar made from wine. The author presents the Hanafi position that vinegar from wine is halal and pure regardless of whether the transformation occurs naturally or artificially (by adding salt, vinegar, moving to sun, etc.). The Shafi'i school holds that if vinegar is produced by adding something (like salt), it remains impure. The Hanafi proof is the hadith 'نعم الإدام الخل' (What an excellent condiment is vinegar) which is general and includes all ways of becoming vinegar. Also, by analogy to slaughtered animals which become pure, the transformation (istihalah) changes the substance from impure to pure. The passage also discusses the purity of the container: the correct view is that the container becomes pure by the transformation of the wine into vinegar. There is a minority view (qil) that it does not become pure due to the disrespect of wine. The text also addresses cases where the vinegar has a bitter taste or predominant acidity – according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, it is permissible if the acidity is predominant; otherwise, not. Finally, it states that if wine is added to broth and cooked, it does not become permissible because it was impure before cooking; similarly, dough kneaded with wine becomes impure.

Key Terms
خل الخمر (khal al-khamr): Vinegar produced from wine, which is considered pure and permissible after transformation.
تخلل (takhallul): The process of wine turning into vinegar, whether naturally or artificially.
استحالة (istihalah): Transformation of one substance into another, changing its legal ruling from impure to pure.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what happens if wine turns into vinegar by adding salt?
A) It remains impure because salt is an external agent.
B) It becomes pure and permissible because transformation occurred.
C) It becomes pure only if the salt is not much.
D) It becomes pure but drinking it is still disliked.

Key Takeaway
Hanafi school permits vinegar from wine in all cases of transformation due to istihalah.

The Permissibility of Vinegar from Wine: Hanafi Position and Proof
Ruling with proof and inter-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on vinegar from wine, identify the Hanafi proof, and explain why the Hanafi school differs from the Shafi'i school on this issue.

Core Idea
Vinegar from wine is pure and permissible, regardless of method – transformation removes impurity.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on vinegar produced from wine, whether spontaneously or through human intervention. The passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq establishes that such vinegar is pure and permissible, a position that contrasts with Imam al-Shafi'i's view. Understanding the proofs and reasoning is essential for grasping Hanafi methodology on impurity removal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act under discussion? Vinegar made from wine. What is the hukm? Mubah (permissible) and tahir (pure).
2
Locate the dalil
The Hanafi proof is the hadith 'Naim illa al-khal' (Yes, except vinegar). This is a general exception from the prohibition of wine.
The hadith is narrated by Abu Dawud, Nasa'i, Ibn Majah.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The word 'illa' (except) excludes vinegar from the ruling of wine, indicating that vinegar from wine is permissible and pure.
The exclusion is absolute, covering all methods of vinegar production.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that vinegar made by adding something (e.g., salt) is not permissible; only spontaneous vinegar is allowed.
The Shafi'i position is based on a different understanding of the hadith and the concept of impurity removal.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The Hanafi school relies on the generality of the hadith and a qiyas (analogy) with slaughtering: just as slaughtering transforms an animal from impure to pure, making vinegar transforms wine from impure to pure.
This tarjih is mufta bihi (relied upon) in the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله: (وخل الخمر سواء خللت أو تخللت) ذلك خل الخمر فلا فرق في بین أن يتخلل بنفسه أو تخلل بإلقاء شيء فيه كالملح أو الخل أو النقل من الظل إلى الشمس أو بإيقاد النار بالقرب منها خلافاً للشافعي إذا تخللت بالقاء شيء فيها كالملح ولنا قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام (نعم) إلا دم الخل» مطلقاً فيتناول جميع صورها ولأن بالتخليل إزالة الوصف المفسد وثبات صفة الصلاح كالذبائح فالتخليل أولى لما فيه من إحراز مال يصير جلالاً ثم فعل ذلك غير حكمه من الحرمة إلى الحل ومن النجاسة إلى الطهارة ألا ترى أن ظرفها كان طاهراً تنجس بها فإذا طهر بالتخليل طهر جميع أجزائه وأجزاء إنائه هو الصحيح. وقيل : لا يطهر لأنه تنجس بإهانة الخمر ولم يوجد ما يوجب طهارته فيبقى على ما كان ولو غسل بالخل فتخلل من ساعته طهر للاستحالة، وكذا إذا صب منه الخمر ثم مليء خلاً يطهر في الحال. وفي المحيط : ولو كان الخل فيه حموضة غالبة وطعم المرارة فإنه لا يحل ما لم تزل من كل وجه وعندهما يحل واعتبر الغالب منها. ولو صب في المرقة خمر فطبخ لم يحل لأنه تنجس قبل الطبخ فلا يحل بالطبخ ولا بحد شاربه لأنه شرب المرق النجس ولو عجن الدقيق بالخمر صار نجساً.
قال رحمه الله : وكره شرب دردي الخمر والامتشاط به لأن فيه أجزاء الخمر فكان حراما نجساً والانتفاع بمثله حرام ولهذا لا يجوز أن يداوي به جرحاً ولا أن يسقي ذمياً ولا صبياً والوبال على من سقاه، وكذا لا يسقيه الدواب، وقيل لا يحمل الخمر إلى من يفسدها ويصيرها خلاً ويحمل ما يفسدها إلى الخمر كما لا يحمل الميتة إلى الكلب، وكذا الدردي في الخل فلا بأس به لأنه خلا لكنه يصير يباح حمل الخمر إليه لا عكسه قال رحمه الله : (ولا يحد شاربه إلا إذا سكر) يعين لا يحد شارب دردي الخمر إلا إذا سكر. وقال الشافعي : يحد شاربه سكر أو لم يسكر لأن الحد يجب في الخمر بشرب قطرة وفي الدردي قطرات . قلنا: وجوب الحد للزجر فيما ترغب النفس فيه وتميل إليه والنفس لا ترغب في شرب الدردي ولا تميل إليه فكان ناقصاً فأشبه غير الخمر من الأشربة فلا يحد ما لم يسكر ودردي الخمر هو التفل. ويكره الاحتقان بالخمر واقطاره في الإحليل لأنه انتفاع بالنجس المحرم.
He (the author) said: (And vinegar from wine, whether you make it vinegar or it becomes vinegar on its own) That is vinegar from wine, so there is no difference between it becoming vinegar by itself or by throwing something into it like salt or vinegar or moving from shade to sun or by lighting fire near it, contrary to al-Shafi'i when it becomes vinegar by throwing something into it like salt. Our proof is his saying (peace be upon him): 'Yes, except blood and vinegar' in absolute terms, so it includes all its forms. And because by making vinegar the corrupt description is removed and the quality of goodness is established, like slaughtered animals, so making vinegar is more appropriate because it preserves property that becomes jalalah. Then that action changed its ruling from prohibition to permissibility and from impurity to purity. Do you not see that its container was pure but became impure by it, so when it is purified by making vinegar, all its parts and the parts of its container become pure. That is the correct view. And it was said: It does not become pure because it became impure by disrespecting wine and nothing was found to necessitate its purity, so it remains as it was. And if it is washed with vinegar and becomes vinegar immediately, it becomes pure due to transformation. Similarly, if wine is poured out from it and then filled with vinegar, it becomes pure immediately. In al-Muhit: And if the vinegar has predominant acidity and a bitter taste, then it is not permissible until it is removed completely, but according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) it is permissible and they consider the predominant. And if wine is poured into soup and then cooked, it does not become permissible because it became impure before cooking, so it is not permissible by cooking. And neither is its drinker punished with hadd because he drank impure soup. And if dough is kneaded with wine, it becomes impure.
He (the author) said: And it is disliked to drink wine dregs and to comb with it because it contains parts of wine, so it is prohibited and impure, and benefiting from such is prohibited. Therefore, it is not permissible to treat a wound with it nor to give it to a dhimmi or a child, and the sin is on the one who gives it. Similarly, one should not give it to animals. And it was said: One should not carry wine to someone who will spoil it and make it vinegar, and one should carry what spoils to the wine, just as one should not carry a carcass to a dog. Similarly, dregs in vinegar are fine because it is vinegar, but it becomes permissible to carry wine to it, not the opposite. He (the author) said: (And its drinker is not punished with hadd unless he becomes intoxicated) meaning: the drinker of wine dregs is not punished with hadd unless he becomes intoxicated. Al-Shafi'i said: Its drinker is punished with hadd whether intoxicated or not, because hadd is obligatory for wine by drinking a drop, and for dregs by drops. We say: The obligation of hadd is for deterrence in what the soul desires and inclines to, and the soul does not desire drinking dregs nor incline to it, so it is deficient, so it resembles non-wine drinks, so he is not punished with hadd unless intoxicated. And wine dregs are the sediment. And it is disliked to use wine as an enema or to instill it into the urethra because it is benefiting from a prohibited impurity.
Source pages p. 404

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq discusses two related topics: vinegar derived from wine, and wine dregs. The author begins by stating the Hanafi ruling that vinegar from wine is pure and permissible regardless of whether it becomes vinegar spontaneously or through human intervention (e.g., adding salt, moving to sun). This is in contrast to Imam al-Shafi'i, who holds that vinegar made by adding something is not permissible. The Hanafi proof is the hadith 'Naim illa dam al-khal' (Yes, except blood and vinegar) which is general, and also a qiyas (analogy) with slaughtering: just as slaughtering transforms an animal from impure to pure, making vinegar transforms wine from impure to pure. The text also mentions a minority view that it does not become pure because of the disrespect shown to wine, but the correct view is that it does. For the method of transformation, if the container was purified by the vinegar, it becomes pure. The text then discusses cases like adding wine to soup—it remains impure and the drinker is not flogged because he drank impure liquid, not wine. Kneading dough with wine makes the dough impure. Next, the author turns to wine dregs (the sediment). Drinking dregs is makruh (disliked) because it contains parts of wine, and using it for any purpose is haram. However, the hadd punishment for drinking dregs is only applied if the drinker becomes intoxicated, contrary to al-Shafi'i who would apply hadd for any amount. The Hanafi reasoning is that hadd is for deterrent on substances the soul desires; since dregs are not desired, the penalty is lighter. The text ends by mentioning the dislike of using wine for enema or urethral instillation.

Key Terms
خل الخمر (khal al-khamr): Vinegar derived from wine, whether spontaneously or by adding an agent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary Hanafi proof for the permissibility of vinegar from wine, regardless of how it was produced?
A) Qiyas on date wine
B) The hadith 'Naim illa al-khal' (Yes, except vinegar)
C) Consensus of the Companions
D) The principle of istihsan

Key Takeaway
Vinegar from wine is pure and permissible in the Hanafi school, whether the transformation occurs spontaneously or through human intervention, based on a general hadith and analogy with slaughtering.

Rulings on Wine Dregs: Impurity and Hadd Punishment
Ruling with proof and inter-madhhab dispute on hadd

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on wine dregs, explain why the hadd punishment for drinking them applies only upon intoxication, and compare this with the Shafi'i position.

Core Idea
Wine dregs are impure and haram, but hadd is only for intoxication because the soul does not desire them.

This lesson examines the legal status of wine dregs (durdiyy al-khamr) – the sediment at the bottom of wine. The passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains that these dregs are impure and cannot be used for any beneficial purpose, including medical treatment. However, the hadd punishment for drinking them is only applied if the drinker becomes intoxicated, a ruling that differs from Imam al-Shafi'i's position. The reasoning lies in the deterrent purpose of hadd and the nature of dregs as undesired by the soul.

Guided Path
1
Identify the object and its ruling
What are wine dregs? The sediment (durdiyy) left after wine. Their ruling: they are impure (najis) and haram to consume or use for any benefit.
The passage uses 'makruh' for drinking, but this is a makruh tahrimi or haram.
2
Understand the prohibition of benefiting
The text prohibits using dregs for combing, treating wounds, feeding dhimmis or children, or giving to animals. This is because they contain parts of wine and are impure.
The prohibition includes any form of benefit or use.
3
Analyze the hadd ruling
The drinker of wine dregs is not punished with hadd unless he becomes intoxicated. This is the Hanafi position.
This contrasts with the Shafi'i view that any amount of dregs triggers hadd.
4
Examine the Hanafi reasoning for hadd
Hadd is for deterrence on substances the soul desires. Since dregs are not desired, the penalty is reduced – it only applies upon intoxication.
The analogy is with other non-wine intoxicating drinks where hadd only applies upon intoxication.
5
Note the Shafi'i ikhtilaf and response
Shafi'i: hadd for any amount because dregs are still wine. Hanafi response: dregs are not desired, so the deterrent purpose is different.
This is a difference in the 'illa (effective cause) for hadd.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
