Conflicting Witnesses on Debt Amount in Rahn: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on testimony conflict in rahn

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Abu Hanifa rejects conflicting testimony on debt amount in a rahn case, contrast this with the Sahibayn's position, and identify the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence).

Core Idea
Conflicting witness testimony fails to prove debt, so the pledge falls — unless the lesser amount is conceded.

When two witnesses testify about a pledge (rahn) but disagree on the loan amount, can their testimony establish the debt — and thereby the pledge? Abu Hanifa says no, because inconsistency undermines proof. The two disciples (Sahibayn) say yes for the lesser amount, since both agree at least that much is owed. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind each position and highlights the practical impact on rahn cases.

Guided Path
1
State the scenario
The mortgagee (murtahin) denies the pledge; two witnesses testify about the debt amount, one says 1,000, the other says 2,000. The debtor does not concede the higher amount.
The key issue is that the witnesses disagree on an essential element: the quantum of debt.
2
Abu Hanifa's position and reasoning
Abu Hanifa holds that the testimony is rejected entirely because the witnesses disagree on the subject matter (amount), making the testimony inconsistent. Without proven debt, the pledge (which is accessory to the debt) cannot be established.
For Abu Hanifa, consistency is a condition for accepting testimony on a fact.
3
Sahibayn's position and reasoning
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that the testimony is accepted to prove the lesser amount (1,000), because both witnesses agree on at least that much. The extra is contested but does not invalidate the common core.
They treat the testimony as partially valid: the shared part is proven, the disputed part is not.
4
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: under Abu Hanifa, the pledge is invalid and the creditor cannot rely on it; under the Sahibayn, the pledge is valid for the lesser amount, so the creditor can enforce it to that extent.
The mufta bihi (relied-upon) position is Abu Hanifa's, as it is more cautious and prevents exploitation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا أنكر المرتهن الرهن فشهدت إحداهما أنه رهنه بألف والأخرى بألفين لا تقبل لأن الدين بهذه الأشياء لم يثبت عند أبي حنيفة رحمه الله لأن اختلاف الشاهدين في المشهود به يمنع قبول الشهادة عنده، وإذا لم يثبت الدين لم يثبت الرهن لأن صحته منوطة بالدين. وعندهما هو رهن بالأقل لأنه يثبت دين ألف بهذه الشهادة عندها إذا كان المدعي يدعي أكثر المالين.
And if the mortgagee denies the pledge, and one of them testifies that he pledged it for a thousand and the other for two thousand, it is not accepted because the debt is not established by these things according to Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, because the disagreement of the two witnesses regarding the subject matter of testimony prevents acceptance of testimony according to him. And if the debt is not established, the pledge is not established because its validity is contingent upon the debt. And according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), it is a pledge for the lesser amount, because a debt of a thousand is established by this testimony when the plaintiff claims the larger of the two amounts.
Source pages p. 443

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding conflicting testimony in a pledge (rahn) case. The scenario: the mortgagee (creditor) denies the existence of the pledge, but two witnesses come forward. One witness says the debtor pledged the collateral for a loan of 1,000 dirhams, and the other says for 2,000 dirhams. The debtor (pledgor) does not concede the higher amount, but the creditors claim the larger sum. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, such testimony is not accepted because the witnesses disagree on the very fact (the amount of debt). Since the debt is not proven, the pledge (which is accessory to the debt) is also not proven. According to the two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani), however, the testimony is accepted to establish the lesser amount (1,000), because both witnesses agree that at least 1,000 is owed, and they only differ on the extra. This is analogous to a plaintiff claiming the larger of two amounts; the testimony is accepted for the lesser.

Key Terms
الرهن (al-rahn): A pledge of property as security for a debt, where the property is held by the creditor until the debt is paid.
المرتهن (al-murtahin): The creditor who receives the pledge as security.
الراهن (al-rahin): The debtor who gives the pledge as security.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, why is the testimony of two witnesses who differ on the amount of the debt (one says 1,000, the other 2,000) not accepted in a rahn case?
A) Because the pledge is invalid without a debt
B) Because the witnesses disagree on the subject matter, making the testimony inadmissible
C) Because the mortgagee denied the pledge
D) Because the debtor claimed a different amount

Key Takeaway
Conflicting witness testimony on debt amount fails to prove the debt, and therefore the pledge (rahn) is not established according to the relied-upon Hanafi position.

Claim of Rahn with Conflicting Evidence on Amount
Ruling on conflicting evidence when mortgagor claims a specific amount

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule of conflicting evidence (tadafu' al-bayyinah) in a rahn dispute to determine which party's claim prevails.

Core Idea
When evidence conflicts, the mortgagee's word rules the secured amount, but the mortgagor's evidence rules the excess.

In a pledge (rahn) dispute, determining which party's claim is stronger can be tricky. The Hanafi jurists have detailed rules for when the mortgagor and mortgagee present conflicting evidence. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains how to resolve such a conflict when the mortgagor's witnesses disagree on the amount. The key is to separate what both parties agree upon from what is contested.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claims of both parties
The mortgagor claims the pledge covers a total of 150 dirhams. He brings two witnesses: one supports 150, the other 100. The mortgagee admits the total debt is 150 but says only 100 is secured by the pledge.
Note the asymmetry: the mortgagee does not contest the total debt, only the secured portion.
2
Separate the agreed amount from the disputed amount
Both parties agree that 100 dirhams is secured. This part is settled by mutual agreement (tasāduq). The remaining 50 is disputed: the mortgagor claims it is also covered, the mortgagee denies it.
3
Apply the rule of 'al-qawl' for the agreed portion
For the 100 dirhams, there is no conflict of evidence because both agree. The mortgagee's statement is accepted without need for evidence (al-qawl li-l-mur'tahin). The pledge is valid for 100.
The mortgagee is the one in possession, and his claim is consistent with the apparent state (ẓāhir al-ḥāl).
4
Apply the rule of 'al-bayyinah' for the disputed portion
For the extra 50, the mortgagor's evidence (one witness for 150) is stronger because it supports his claim of a larger securement. The mortgagee has no evidence for his denial. Thus, the mortgagor's evidence prevails for the excess.
The Hanafi rule is that the party who seeks to prove an additional obligation bears the burden of proof. Here, the mortgagor is trying to prove that the mortgagee holds the pledge for a larger debt.
5
Derive the final hukm
The property is a valid pledge for 100 dirhams by mutual agreement. The mortgagor's evidence for the extra 50 is stronger, so he can pursue that amount separately if he has evidence, but it does not automatically make the pledge cover 150 unless proven.
The practical outcome: the mortgagee must return the property upon repayment of 100, but the mortgagor can claim the extra 50 in a separate action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ادعى الراهن الرهن بمائة وخمسين وهي قيمته وشهد أحدهما بذلك والآخر بمائة وقال المرتهن عليه مائة وخمسون وهذا رهن بمائة منها فالقول للمرتهن والبينة للراهن لأنه يثبت الدين وهو وخمسون لتصادقهما عليه لا بالبينة وتصادقا أن العين رهن بمائة فصار رهناً بمائة بتصادقهما مائة على ذلك إلا أن بينة الراهن أكثر إثباتاً لأنه يثبت زيادة إيفاء على المرتهن
If the mortgagor claims the pledge for one hundred and fifty, and that is its value, and one witness testifies to that and the other to one hundred, and the mortgagee says, 'He owes me one hundred and fifty, and this is a pledge for one hundred of it,' then the statement is the mortgagee's and the evidence is the mortgagor's, because he establishes the debt, which is fifty (extra), because they both agree on it, not by evidence. And they both agree that the property is a pledge for one hundred, so it becomes a pledge for one hundred by their mutual agreement, except that the mortgagor's evidence is more establishing because it establishes an excess payment upon the mortgagee.
Source pages p. 443

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific scenario in rahn disputes. The mortgagor claims that the property was pledged for 150 dirhams, which is its market value. He brings one witness who testifies to 150, and another witness who testifies to 100. The mortgagee admits that the total debt is 150 but claims that only 100 of it is secured by the pledge. The ruling: the mortgagee's statement is accepted regarding the pledge being for 100 only, because both parties agree that at least 100 is secured. However, the mortgagor's evidence (the witness for 150) is stronger regarding the excess 50, because he aims to prove an additional obligation on the mortgagee (i.e., that the mortgagee holds the pledge for the full 150). The practical outcome: the property is considered a pledge for 100 by mutual agreement, but the mortgagor may pursue the extra 50 through other means if he has evidence.

Key Terms
القول (al-qawl): The statement of a party that is accepted in the absence of strong counter-evidence; here, the mortgagee's word is taken.
البينة (al-bayyinah): Evidence, typically two witnesses or other proof; here, the mortgagor's evidence is considered stronger for the extra amount.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the scenario where the mortgagor claims a pledge for 150 but has one witness for 150 and another for 100, and the mortgagee admits a total debt of 150 but says only 100 is secured, which part of the claim is decided by the mortgagee's statement?
A) The entire debt of 150
B) The pledge securing 100
C) The excess 50
D) The value of the property

Key Takeaway
In a rahn dispute with conflicting evidence, the mortgagee's word determines the mutually agreed securement, but the mortgagor's evidence determines any excess he claims.

Conflicting Evidence: Wadi'a vs Rahn
Ruling on conflicting evidence between wadi'a and rahn.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when evidence conflicts between a claim of deposit (wadi'a) and a claim of pledge (rahn), and explain the reasoning based on the binding nature and liability of the pledge.

Core Idea
In a dispute, the pledgee's evidence prevails because rahn is binding and includes liability, unlike wadi'a.

When two parties present conflicting evidence—one claiming an item was deposited, the other claiming it was pledged—the Hanafi school provides a clear rule. This lesson examines the reasoning behind preferring the mortgagee's evidence, rooted in the legal characteristics of the two contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conflict
Recognize the scenario: one party claims the property was a deposit (amanah), the other claims it was a pledge (rahn). Both have evidence.
2
State the ruling
The Hanafi ruling: the evidence of the mortgagee (pledgee) is accepted.
This is the mufta bihi position.
3
Analyze the first reason
A pledge is binding (lazim) and involves liability (daman), whereas a deposit is non-binding and has no liability unless negligence. This gives the pledgee's claim greater legal weight.
Binding nature means the contract cannot be dissolved unilaterally.
4
Analyze the second reason
It is possible to reconcile both statements by assuming the owner first deposited the item and later pledged it, because a pledge can validly follow a deposit. The reverse (deposit after pledge) would require the mortgagee's consent.
This reconciliation supports the plausibility of the mortgagee's claim.
5
Apply the rule
In practice, when a judge faces such conflicting evidence, the mortgagee's testimony is preferred due to the stronger legal consequences and the logical sequence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقام البينة أنه استودعه وهو أقام البينة أنه ارتهنه تقبل بينة المرتهن لأن الرهن جاء لازماً وفيه ضمان ولا لزوم ولا ضمان في الوديعة فكانت بينة الراهن أكثر إثباتاً، ولأنه أمكن العمل بالبينتين بأن يجعل كأنه أودعه ثم رهنه لأن الرهن يرد على الإيداع وأما الإيداع لا يرد على الرهن إلا برضا المرتهن
One presented evidence that he deposited it, and the other presented evidence that he took it as a pledge. The evidence of the mortgagee is accepted because the pledge is binding and involves liability, and there is no binding nor liability in the deposit. So the evidence of the mortgagor is more establishing? And because it is possible to act on both pieces of evidence by making it as if he deposited it then pledged it, because the pledge can occur after the deposit, but the deposit cannot occur after the pledge except with the consent of the mortgagee.
Source pages p. 443

Commentary
This passage resolves a conflict where one party (the depositor) claims that the property was given as a deposit (wadi'a), while the other (the mortgagee) claims it was given as a pledge (rahn). The ruling is that the mortgagee's evidence is accepted. The reasoning: a pledge is a binding contract that imposes liability on the pledgee (the mortgagee is responsible for loss), whereas a deposit is not binding (the depositary may return it at any time) and normally involves no liability for loss unless there is negligence. Additionally, it is possible to reconcile both claims by considering that the owner first deposited the item and later pledged it, since a pledge can validly be made on an item already in deposit, but the reverse (deposit after pledge) would require the mortgagee's consent. Therefore, the mortgagee's claim is preferred as it is more consistent with the legal nature of the transaction.

Key Terms
الوديعة (al-wadi'a): A deposit of property for safekeeping without compensation.
الضمان (al-daman): Liability for loss or damage; in rahn, the pledgee is liable if the property is lost through his fault, but in wadi'a, the depositary is not liable unless negligent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a dispute where one party brings evidence of deposit and the other of pledge, why is the pledgee's evidence preferred?
A) Because the pledge occurs first
B) Because the pledge is binding and involves liability, unlike deposit
C) Because the depositor has no witnesses
D) Because the pledgee is more trustworthy

Key Takeaway
In conflicting claims of deposit and pledge, the Hanafi school prefers the pledgee's evidence because a pledge is binding and involves liability, and it allows reconciliation as a later pledge over a prior deposit.

Conflicting Evidence: Pledge vs. Sale and Gift
Ruling on conflicting evidence of pledge vs. sale/gift

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for preferring evidence of sale over evidence of pledge and explain the two reasons for this preference.

Core Idea
Sale transfers ownership immediately and is binding on both sides, so its evidence prevails over pledge.

When two parties present conflicting evidence—one claiming a property was pledged and the other claiming it was sold—the Hanafi school gives precedence to the sale. Why? Because a sale creates immediate ownership and is binding on both parties, whereas a pledge only grants a right of retention and is not binding on the creditor. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and also extends the same rule to gifts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Conflicting Claims
The passage presents a scenario where one party (the mortgagor) claims the property was pledged (rahn), and the other party (the mortgagee) claims it was sold (bay'). The ruling: it is judged as a sale.
The same applies if the mortgagee claims a gift (hibah) with possession.
2
Understand the First Reason: Nature of the Contracts
Sale is binding (lazim) from both sides—the seller cannot revoke, and the buyer acquires ownership immediately. Pledge is not binding from the mortgagee's side; he can return the pledge and demand another security. Sale gives immediate ownership (tamlik al-ayn), while pledge only gives a right of retention (haqs al-habs). Therefore, the evidence of sale is 'more establishing' (akthar ithbatan).
Ownership is a stronger right than mere security.
3
Understand the Second Reason: Reconciliation of Evidence
Both pieces of evidence can be reconciled by assuming the debtor first pledged the property and later sold it to the mortgagee. This works because a sale can occur after a pledge (the debtor still owns the property subject to the pledge). But a pledge cannot occur after a sale (the seller no longer owns the property). Thus, the sequence that respects both claims is: first pledge, then sale.
The principle: al-bay' yaridu 'ala al-rahn wa al-rahn la yaridu 'ala al-bay'.
4
Extend to Gift
If the mortgagee claims a gift (hibah) with possession (qabd), the evidence of gift is taken, because a gift also transfers ownership immediately, like a sale.
Gift must be accompanied by possession to be binding in Hanafi law.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الراهن أقام البينة على الرهن والآخر على البيع جعل بيعاً لأن البيع لازم من الجانبين وارهن غير لازم من جانب المرتهن والبيع يوجب الملك للحال والرهن لا فكانت بينة البيع أكثر إثباتاً، ولأنه أمكن العمل بالبينتين بأن تجعل كأنه رهن أولاً ثم باع لأن البيع يرد على الرهن والرهن لا يرد على البيع، وكذلك لو ادعى المرتهن الهبة والقبض يؤخذ ببينة الهبة لأن الهبة توجب الملك للحال كالبيع
If the mortgagor presents evidence of pledge and the other presents evidence of sale, it is judged as a sale, because sale is binding from both sides while the pledge is not binding from the side of the mortgagee, and sale gives ownership immediately and the pledge does not. So the evidence of sale is more establishing, and because it is possible to act on both pieces of evidence by considering that he first pledged and then sold, because sale can occur after pledge but pledge cannot occur after sale. Similarly, if the mortgagee claims a gift with possession, the evidence of gift is taken, because gift gives ownership immediately like sale.
Source pages p. 443

Commentary
This passage deals with conflicting evidence between a claim of pledge (rahn) and a claim of sale (bay'), or between pledge and gift (hibah). The ruling: the evidence of sale is preferred over evidence of pledge. The reasoning: a sale is a binding contract from both sides (the seller cannot unilaterally revoke, and the buyer acquires ownership immediately), whereas a pledge is not binding from the mortgagee's side (the creditor can return the pledge and demand another form of security). Sale transfers ownership instantly, while pledge only gives a right of retention. Additionally, the two claims can be reconciled by assuming the debtor first pledged the property and later sold it to the mortgagee, because a sale can validly take place after a pledge (the buyer can buy the pledged property from the owner), but a pledge cannot be validly made after a sale (since the property is no longer owned by the pledgor). The same rule applies if the claim is a gift with possession (hibah ma'a al-qabd), because a gift also transfers ownership immediately like a sale. Therefore, the evidence of sale or gift is considered more establishing and is given precedence.

Key Terms
البيع (al-bay'): A contract of sale that transfers ownership of property for a price.
الهبة (al-hibah): Gift, transfer of ownership without compensation.
الرهن (al-rahn): Pledge or mortgage; property given as security for a debt, with the creditor having the right of retention but not ownership.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a conflict between evidence of a pledge and evidence of a sale, which evidence is preferred and why?
A) Pledge, because it involves a debt
B) Sale, because it is binding from both sides and transfers ownership immediately
C) Pledge, because it came first
D) Neither, both are rejected

Key Takeaway
When pledge and sale (or gift) conflict, the evidence of sale or gift prevails because it establishes immediate ownership and is binding on both parties.

Conflicting Claims: Purchase with Possession vs Pledge with Possession
Resolving conflicting claims through possession and priority

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule for resolving conflicting claims of purchase and pledge when both parties possess the property, including the role of apparent possession and priority.

Core Idea
The claim matching apparent possession prevails, but prior established rights are preserved.

This lesson addresses a common conflict in property disputes: when one claims to have bought a property and taken possession, and another claims to have taken it as a pledge and also has possession. The Hanafi school provides a nuanced ruling that balances the evidentiary value of possession with the need to honor prior rights. We will explore the reasoning and its application through an analogy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and their claims
Recognize that one party claims purchase with possession, the other claims pledge with possession. Both assert a right over the same property.
Assume both claims are backed by some evidence (e.g., witnesses).
2
Examine the possession: where is the property located?
Determine whether the property is in the hands of the mortgagor (debtor) or another party. This is crucial for the default ruling.
The passage specifically addresses when the property is in the mortgagor's hand.
3
Apply the principle of apparent possession
If the property is with the mortgagor, the purchase claim is preferred because the apparent state suggests ownership. The mortgagee's possession is not apparent in this scenario.
The principle 'al-yad hujjah' (possession is evidence) is applied.
4
Check for prior knowledge of the pledge
If it is known (by proof or admission) that the pledge preceded the purchase, the ruling changes: the pledge claim prevails. The mortgagee's actual possession is then recognized.
Knowledge can be through evidence or admission; mere doubt does not overturn the default.
5
Apply the analogy to two buyers
The passage draws an analogy: if two people claim to have bought from the same seller and one has actual possession, the possessor is preferred. This reinforces that actual possession is a strong indicator of right.
The common illa (effective cause) is actual possession (قبض معاين).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ادعى الشراء والقبض والآخر ادعى الرهن والقبض يحكم بالشراء إذا كان في يد الراهن، فإن علم بتقدم الرهن جعل رهناً لأن للمرتهن قبضاً معايناً ولا ينقض بالشك كما لو ادعيا الشراء من واحد ولأحدهما قبض معاين وأقاما البينة فصاحب القبض أولى
If one claims purchase and possession, and the other claims pledge and possession, it is judged as purchase if the property is in the hand of the mortgagor. Then if the precedence of the pledge is known, it is made a pledge, because the mortgagee has actual possession and it is not invalidated by doubt, just as if both claim to have bought from one person and one has actual possession and they both present evidence, the possessor is given precedence.
Source pages p. 443

Commentary
This passage resolves a conflict where two parties each claim a right over the same property based on different transactions, both backed by evidence and actual possession. Specifically, one party claims to have purchased the property and taken possession; the other claims to have taken it as a pledge (rahn) and also holds possession. The ruling: if the property is in the hands of the mortgagor (i.e., the debtor), the claim of purchase is accepted, because the apparent state (the property is with the debtor) suggests ownership, not pledge. However, if it is known (by evidence or admission) that the pledge preceded the purchase, then it is treated as a pledge, because the mortgagee's possession is actual and established. This is analogous to a case where two people both claim to have bought from the same seller, and one has actual possession; the one with possession is preferred because his possession is visible and carries weight. The principle is that actual possession is a strong indication of right, and it cannot be overturned by mere doubt.

Key Terms
القبض (al-qabd): Possession; actual physical control over property.
يد (yad): Hand or possession; a person in possession has a presumptive right.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a dispute between a purchaser in possession and a mortgagee in possession of the same property, which claim is preferred if the property is found in the hands of the mortgagor?
A) The mortgagee's claim, because pledge is stronger
B) The purchaser's claim, because the property is with the mortgagor
C) Both are equal
D) The mortgagee, if he has witnesses

Key Takeaway
In conflicts between purchase and pledge claims, the ruling favors the claim that matches the apparent possession, but prior established rights take precedence.

Testimony of Mortgagors That Property Belongs to Another
Ruling on the inadmissibility of mortgagors' testimony against their own pledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the two reasons (shubhah and iqrar) why the testimony of mortgagors against their own pledge is rejected and apply the principle to a similar scenario.

Core Idea
A witness with personal interest (shubhah) or whose testimony harms a third party's right is rejected — even if truthful.

When two mortgagors testify that the property they pledged actually belongs to a third party, Islamic law rejects their testimony. This lesson examines the dual rationale: first, the mortgagors stand to benefit from invalidating the mortgage, creating a suspicion (shubhah) that undermines their credibility; second, their testimony amounts to an admission that would unfairly harm the mortgagee's established right. Understanding this ruling helps protect contractual rights from self-serving testimony.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its application
What is the specific scenario? Two mortgagors testify that the mortgaged property belongs to someone else. The ruling: the testimony is not accepted (la tuqbal). Identify the legal effect on the mortgagee's right.
2
Analyze the first reason: benefit (shubhah)
The text states that the mortgagors 'draw benefit and gain' by invalidating the mortgage. How does this create shubhah (suspicion) in their testimony? Consider that any witness with a direct personal interest is considered unreliable in Islamic evidence law.
Shubhah is a general principle affecting witness credibility.
3
Analyze the second reason: testimony as admission (iqrar)
The text equates the testimony to an admission by the mortgagors against themselves. Why is this testimony not valid with respect to the mortgagee? Because an admission cannot bind a third party whose right is already established.
The analogy is to an explicit admission (iqrar sarih).
4
Synthesize the dual reasoning
Combine both reasons to see why the testimony is rejected. Note that even if the testimony were true, it is procedurally invalid because of the witness's interest and the infringement on a third party's right.
This shows that truthfulness alone does not guarantee admissibility.
5
Apply the principle to a new scenario
Consider: A debtor testifies that he already repaid a loan, but the creditor denies it. Does the testimony bind the creditor? Apply the same logic: the debtor benefits from the testimony, and it would harm the creditor's right. The testimony is not accepted against the creditor.
This illustrates the broader rule beyond mortgage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو شهد الراهنان بأن المرهون ملك آخر لا تقبل لأنهما بهذه الشهادة يجران لأنفسهما نفعاً ومغنماً لأنهما يريدان إبطال حق المرتهن عن الرهن عليهما، وفي إبطال حق المرتهن عن الرهن نفع لهما في الجملة فتمكنت الشبهة في شهادتهما فلا تقبل ولأن هذه الشهادة في معنى الإقرار لأنهما يشهدان على أنفسهما لأنهما يسعيان في نقض عقد قديم وشهادة الإنسان على نفسه إقرار فهذا إقرار يتضمن إبطال حق المرتهن فلا يصح في حق المرتهن كما لو أقر صريحاً
And if the two mortgagors testify that the mortgaged property belongs to someone else, it is not accepted because by this testimony they draw benefit and gain to themselves, because they wish to invalidate the mortgagee's right of pledge against them, and in invalidating the mortgagee's right of pledge there is benefit for them in general, so doubt is attached to their testimony, thus it is not accepted. And because this testimony is in the meaning of an admission, since they testify against themselves because they are seeking to revoke an old contract, and one's testimony against oneself is an admission, so this is an admission that entails invalidating the mortgagee's right, so it is not valid with regard to the mortgagee, just as if they explicitly admitted.
Source pages p. 443

Commentary
This passage addresses the case where the pledgors (mortgagors) themselves testify that the property they pledged actually belongs to a third party, not to them. Such testimony is rejected on two grounds. First, the mortgagors stand to benefit from this testimony: if accepted, it would invalidate the pledge and release their property from the mortgagee's claim. Since they have a personal interest, their testimony is tainted by suspicion (shubhah) and is therefore inadmissible. Second, their testimony is equivalent to an admission against themselves, because they are essentially revoking their own prior contract. In Islamic law, one's testimony against oneself is effectively an admission (iqrar). However, an admission that harms the rights of a third party (here the mortgagee) is not valid against that party. Just as a clear admission by the mortgagors that the property belongs to another would not affect the mortgagee's right, so their testimony to the same effect is not accepted. The mortgagee's right, once established through the original contract, cannot be defeated by the mortgagors' self-serving statements.

Key Terms
الشبهة (al-shubhah): Suspicion or doubt that arises when a witness has a personal interest in the outcome, rendering testimony inadmissible.
الإقرار (al-iqrar): Admission against oneself; here, the testimony is treated as an admission, but it does not bind the mortgagee.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the testimony of the mortgagors that the pledged property belongs to someone else rejected?
A) Because they are not reliable
B) Because they benefit from invalidating the pledge, and it is like an admission against the mortgagee's right
C) Because the mortgagee did not consent
D) Because the property is already pledged

Key Takeaway
The testimony of a mortgagor that the pledged property belongs to another is rejected because they have a personal interest (shubhah) and it acts as an admission that cannot bind the mortgagee.
